Divorce of the Intoxicated: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Explaining rulings on divorce by intoxicated or mute persons with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the divorce of an intoxicated person, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the conditions under which the Hanafi school differentiates between sinful and non-sinful intoxication.

Core Idea
A drunkard's divorce is valid if he is blameworthy; the Shafi'i analogy with the sleeper fails because the drunkard's condition is self-inflicted.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of divorce pronounced by an intoxicated person. The Hanafi school holds that a drunkard's divorce takes effect as a deterrent, while Imam al-Shafi'i argues it is invalid by analogy with a sleeper. Understanding the underlying principles and the Hanafi distinction between sinful and non-sinful intoxication is crucial for correctly applying the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What is the ruling on a drunkard's divorce according to the majority of Hanafis? Note the key exception for non-sinful intoxication.
The majority view (mufta bihi) is that divorce occurs if due to sin.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Identify Imam al-Shafi'i's position and the evidence he uses (qiyas on the sleeper from the hadith 'the pen is lifted').
3
Follow the opponent's qiyas
How does the Shafi'i argument analogize the drunkard to the sleeper? What is the underlying illa?
Illa: loss of consciousness. Sleeper's divorce is void; drunkard is worse, so a fortiori void.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Why do Hanafis reject the qiyas? What distinguishes the drunkard from the sleeper? Also note the Quranic evidence cited.
Hanafis argue the drunkard is legally addressed (Quran 4:43) and blameworthy, unlike the sleeper.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طائع أو مكره، أو أخرس بإشارته المعهودة. قوله: خلافاً للشافعي؛ فإنّه لا يقع عنده طلاق السكران؛ لأنه أسوء من النائم، فإذا لم يقع طلاقُهُ لحديث: «رفع القلمُ عن ثلاث، وفيه: وعن النائم حتى يستيقظ»، فلأن لا يقعُ طلاقه أولى، وهذا هو مختار بعض مشايخنا كالكرخي والطحاوي، وأكثرهم قالوا بوقوع طلاق السكران، زجراً له على سكره.
(2) وهو من يزول عقله فلا يميز الأشياء عن بعضها بأن لا يعرف الرجل من المرأة، ولا السماء من الأرض، ولا الطول من العرض كما في حاشية التبيين (3): 194، وله حالان: 1. أن يكون ذلك بسبب معصية كما إذا تناول الشخص شيئاً محرماً طائعاً مختاراً سواء كان خمراً، أو نبيذاً، أو حشيشاً، أو أفيوناً، فسكر، وطلّق زوجته وقع عليها الطلاق؛ لأنه مخاطب شرعاً بقوله: {يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَقْرَبُوا الصَّلَاةَ وَأَنتُمْ سُكَارَى} [النساء: 43]، فوجب نفوذ تصرفه؛ لأن عقله زال بسبب هو معصية، فيجعل باقياً زجراً له. وأما البنج فإنه إن كان للتداوي لم يقع لعدم المعصية، وإن كان للهو وإدخال الآفة قصداً فإنه يقع زجراً على المفتى به، كما في رد المحتار (3: 240). 2. أن يكون بغير معصية كمَن شرب دواء مسكراً تعين فيه الشفاء بوصف الطبيب العدل الماهر أو اضطر بأن غص بلقمة وخاف الموت ولم يجد ما يسيغها إلا الخمر، أو أكره على تعاطي ما يسكر فتعاطاه، فسكر وطلّق زوجته فلا يقع. ينظر: سبل الوفاق (ص249-250)، وشرح الأحكام الشرعية (1: 197-198)، والتبيين (2: 196)، ورد المحتار (3: 240)، وغيرها.
أن تدوم العقلة إلى وقت الموت على المفتى به، كما في درر الحكام (2: 430)، والشرنبلالية (2: 430)، والدر المختار (2: 425)، والبحر الرائق (8: 544). وقدره التمرتاشي بسنة، وقال صاحب مجمع الضمانات (ص455) أنه ضعيف. قال ابن عابدين في رد المحتار (2: 425): وكذا لو تزوج بالإشارة لا يحل له وطؤها لعدم نفاذه قبل الموت وكذا سائر عقوده، ولا يخفى ما في هذا من الحرج، ثم قال: وفي التتارخانية عن الينابيع (ق98/ب): ويقع طلاق الأخرس بالإشارة، ويريد به الذي ولد وهو أخرس أو طرأ عليه ذلك ودام حتى صارت إشارته مفهومة وإلا لم يعتبر.
(A divorcer) willingly or coerced, or a mute with his understood gesture. He said: contrary to al-Shafi'i; for with him the divorce of an intoxicated person does not occur because he is worse than a sleeping person. Since the divorce of the sleeping person does not occur due to the hadith: 'The pen is lifted from three, and among them: the sleeper until he wakes up,' then the divorce of the drunkard is even more worthy of not occurring. This is the choice of some of our scholars like al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi, but most of them said that the divorce of the drunkard does occur, as a deterrent to his drunkenness. (Footnote) He is one whose mind departs so he cannot distinguish things from each other, not knowing a man from a woman, nor the sky from the earth, nor length from width, as in Hashiyat al-Tabyin. He has two states: 1. That it occurs due to sin, as when a person willingly consumes something prohibited, whether wine, nabidh, hashish, or opium, gets drunk, and divorces his wife – the divorce occurs because he is addressed by the law in His saying: {O you who believe, do not approach prayer while you are intoxicated} [4:43]. So his action is binding because his mind left due to a sin, so it is considered remaining as a deterrent. As for bang (narcotic) if for treatment it does not occur for lack of sin, and if for amusement and deliberate harm then it occurs as a deterrent according to the relied-upon position. 2. That it occurs without sin, like someone who drinks an intoxicating medicine prescribed by a just skilled doctor, or is forced to swallow food and fears death and finds only wine to swallow it, or is coerced to consume an intoxicant and does so, then is drunk and divorces – it does not occur. (Continuation) The continuity of the mental defect until the time of death is the relied-upon position. Al-Timurtashi estimated it as one year, but the author of Majma' al-Damanat said it is weak. Ibn Abidin said: Likewise if he marries by gesture, it is not permissible for him to consummate due to its non-execution before death, and so forth for his other contracts – the difficulty in this is clear. Then he said: In al-Tatarikhaniyya from al-Yanabi': The divorce of a mute falls by gesture, and intends thereby one who was born mute or it occurred and continued until his gesture became understood, otherwise it is not considered.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the validity of divorce pronounced by an intoxicated person. The commentator first states the ruling of the matn: a divorcer may be willing, coerced, or a mute using his understood gesture. Then he addresses the Shafi'i position, which holds that a drunkard's divorce does not occur, analogizing him to a sleeper whose divorce is invalid by the hadith 'The pen is lifted from three...' The Hanafi school has two opinions: some early scholars like al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi sided with Shafi'i, but the majority of Hanafis rule that the divorce does occur as a deterrent (zajran) against the sin of intoxication. The detailed footnote elaborates on the definition of the drunkard (one whose mind is completely gone) and distinguishes two cases: (1) if the intoxication is due to sin (like consuming alcohol or drugs willingly), the divorce falls and is effective, because the person is legally addressed and his loss of reason is due to his own transgression; (2) if the intoxication is without sin (e.g., consuming medicine under doctor's advice, or being forced), the divorce does not fall. This is the Hanafi position: the drunkard's divorce is valid only when he is blameworthy. The passage then discusses the mute's divorce by gesture, stating that his understood gesture (al-ishara al-ma'huda) is accepted as valid expression of divorce, based on istihsan. The footnote clarifies conditions: the gesture must be consistently understandable, and if the mute can write, some scholars like Ibn al-Humam prefer writing over gesture. The discussion concludes with the requirement that the mute's condition (the inability to speak) be permanent until death for the gesture to be relied upon.

Key Terms
سكران (sakran): One whose mind is completely gone due to intoxication, unable to distinguish things.
أخرس (akhras): A mute person who cannot speak; his divorce via understood gesture is valid.
إشارة معهودة (ishāra maʿhūda): A gesture that is customary and understood by others, indicating a clear meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The divorce does not occur, by analogy with the sleeper.
B) The divorce occurs and is valid as a deterrent.
C) The divorce occurs only if he was aware of his actions.
D) The divorce is suspended until he becomes sober.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a drunkard's divorce is valid if his intoxication was sinful, as a deterrent; otherwise, it is not valid.

Invalid Divorce from Minors, Insane, and Master; Number of Divorces for Free and Slave Women
Stating the invalidity of divorce from certain persons and the number of divorces for free and slave women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which persons' divorces are invalid in Hanafi fiqh and identify the prescribed number of divorces for free and slave women.

Core Idea
Divorce is invalid from a minor, insane, sleeping, or master over a slave's wife; free women have three divorces, slave women two.

The matn of this lesson lists four categories of individuals whose pronouncement of divorce has no legal effect: a minor, an insane person, a sleeping person, and a master divorcing his slave's wife. The commentary explains the reasoning — lack of legal capacity or ownership of the marriage — and then establishes the fixed number of divorces for free and slave women, with a brief note about cases where the husband's status differs from the wife's.

Guided Path
1
Identify the excluded persons
Read the opening of the matn: 'لا طلاق صبي ومجنون ونائم وسيد على زوجة عبده'. List the four persons whose divorce is invalid: a minor (sabī), an insane person (majnūn), a sleeping person (nāʾim), and a master (sayyid) over his slave's wife.
2
Determine the legal basis for each exclusion
For each category, identify the reason: the minor lacks legal capacity (ahliyya al-adaʾ), the insane lacks intellect, the sleeping lacks consciousness, and the master does not own the marriage of his slave's wife. The commentary emphasizes that divorce can only be pronounced by the one who owns the marriage, i.e., the husband.
3
Analyze the master's position using the hadith
The commentary cites the hadith 'إنما الطلاق من أخذ الساق' (Indeed divorce is from the one who takes the calf) to support that only the husband (the one who 'takes charge' of the marriage) can divorce. Even though the hadith is weak, the Hanafi school relies on the principle that the master is not a party to the marriage.
The hadith is reported by Ibn Majah, al-Daraqutni, and others, but considered weak by many scholars. However, the ruling is supported by other evidence.
4
Parse the number of divorces for free and slave women
The matn continues: 'وطلاق الحرة والأمة ثلاثة واثنان'. A free woman has three revocable divorces; a slave woman has two. This is a fixed ruling regardless of the husband's status.
5
Interpret the condition 'ولو زوجهما خلافهما'
This phrase means 'even if their husbands are the opposite of them', i.e., a free woman married to a slave still has three divorces, and a slave woman married to a free man still has two. The commentary mentions four scenarios but only details the first (free husband + free wife). The other three are implied: free husband with slave wife (two divorces), slave husband with free wife (three divorces), slave husband with slave wife (two divorces).
The listing of four scenarios is incomplete in this passage; the remaining scenarios are likely on subsequent pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا طلاق صبي، ومجنون، ونائم، وسيد على زوجة عبده. وطلاق الحرة، والأمة ثلاثة واثنان، ولو زوجهما خلافهما.
قوله: وسيد؛ يعني إنّ طَلَّقَ سيّد زوجة عبد لا يقعُ طلاقه، فإنّ الطلاق لا يملكه إلا من يملكها بالنكاح، وإن هو إلا العبد، ويدلّ عليه حديث: «إنّما الطلاق من أخذ الساق).
قوله: وطلاق الحرة ... الخ؛ أشار به إلى أنّ في المتن لف ونشر مرتباً، واختاره للاختصار.
قوله: ولو زوجهما؛ الواو الوصلية؛ أي ولو كان زوج الحرّة عبداً، وزوج الأمة حراً، فهاهنا أربع صور:
زوج حر مع زوجته وفيها يملك عليها الثلاث، ولا تبين بينونة مغلظة، يحتاج إلى التحليل إلا بعد الطلاق الثالث.
(1) في سنن ابن ماجة (1: 362)، وسنن الدارقطني (37)، والمعجم الكبير (11: 300)، والكامل (6: 11)، وسنن البيهقي الكبير (370)، وقال البيهقي وابن حجر في تلخيص الحبير (3: 216)، والهيثمي في مجمع الزوائد (4: 334)، والكناني في مصباح الزجاجة (2: 131): ضعيف. وينظر: كشف الخفاء (1: 248)، والدراية (2: 199)، ونصب الراية (4: 165)، وخلاصة البدر المنير (2: 228)، وغيرها.
There is no divorce (valid) from a minor, an insane person, a sleeping person, and a master over the wife of his slave. And the divorce of a free woman and a slave woman is three and two respectively, even if their husbands are the opposite of them. He said: 'And a master': meaning that if a master divorces the wife of his slave, his divorce does not occur, because divorce is only owned by one who owns her through marriage, and that is only the slave. The hadith indicates it: 'Indeed divorce is from the one who takes the calf (i.e., the husband).' He said: 'And the divorce of the free woman... etc.': He indicated that in the matn there is a well-arranged 'laff wa nashr' (splendid arrangement), and he chose it for brevity. He said: 'And if their husbands are opposite': the 'waw' is conjunctive; meaning even if the husband of the free woman is a slave, and the husband of the slave woman is a free man. So here there are four scenarios: a free husband with his wife – in this case he owns three divorces over her, and she does not separate by a severe separation (bainuna mughallaza) requiring tahlil except after the third divorce. (Footnote) In Sunan Ibn Majah... etc. – it is weak.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement (matn) followed by commentary. The matn states that divorce is invalid from a minor, an insane person, a sleeping person, and a master over his slave's wife. The reason is that these persons lack legal capacity or ownership of the marriage. The commentary explains that the master does not own the marriage of his slave's wife; only the slave husband does. The hadith 'Innama al-talaq min akhadha al-saq' (reported but weak) supports this. The second part of the matn states that a free woman has three revocable divorces, and a slave woman has two. This is a general rule regardless of the husband's status. The commentary clarifies that the matn uses 'laff wa nashr' (a stylistic device) to pair the two types of women with the number of divorces. Then it addresses the case when the husband's status differs: if the husband is a slave and the wife is free, the marriage contracts are valid, but the husband still owns only three divorces? Actually the commentary says: 'even if their husbands are opposite' – meaning if the free woman’s husband is a slave, and the slave woman’s husband is a free man. The four pictures are outlined: (1) free husband with free wife – he owns three divorces; (2) free husband with slave wife – he owns two divorces; (3) slave husband with free wife – he owns three divorces? Actually the text only lists the first picture (free husband with free wife). The rest are likely on subsequent pages. The commentary indicates four pictures but only gives one. The hadith used for the master's divorce is cited as weak by several scholars. The relied-upon ruling is that a master’s divorce of his slave’s wife is invalid.

Key Terms
صبي (sabi): A minor below the age of puberty whose divorce is invalid.
سيد (sayyid): Master of a slave; his divorce of his slave's wife is invalid.
طلاق الحرة (talāq al-ḥurra): The divorce of a free woman; she has three divorces.
طلاق الأمة (talāq al-ama): The divorce of a slave woman; she has two divorces.

C) One
D) Unlimited

Key Takeaway
Divorce is invalid from minors, insane, sleeping, and masters over slave wives; free women have three divorces, slave women have two.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
12 min
Divorce Number: Wife's Status vs. Husband's Status (Hanafi vs. Shafi'i)
Commentary on matn: no divorce from minor/insane/sleeping/master; numbers for free/slave women; inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that the number of divorces is based on the wife's status, contrast it with the Shafi'i position, and apply the ruling to scenarios with different free/slave statuses.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the number of divorces is determined by the wife's status, not the husband's.

This lesson examines a key disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the permissible number of divorces when the spouses differ in free/slave status. The source text from Sharh al-Wiqayah presents the Hanafi view grounded in a hadith and reasoning, while the Shafi'i position relies on a different tradition. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for correctly applying divorce rulings in mixed-status marriages.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi rule: the number of divorces a husband can issue is based on the wife's status (free or slave), regardless of his own status.
Free woman: three divorces; slave woman: two divorces.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the number depends on the husband's status, not the wife's.
Thus, a free husband marrying a slave woman would have three divorces according to Shafi'i, not two.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i evidence: the mawquf hadith 'Divorce is by men, and the waiting period by women' from Ibn Abbas and others.
This suggests that the capacity for divorce attaches to the husband.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school counters with a marfu' hadith: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles.' Though its chain is weak, Ibn al-Humam considered it hasan, and scholars have acted upon it. They also argue that slavery diminishes the blessing of dissolution, so the wife's capacity is relevant.
The analogy of 'iddah (waiting period) further supports the wife-based criterion: the 'iddah of a slave woman is half that of a free woman.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: if a free man marries a slave woman, the Hanafi school allows only two divorces, while the Shafi'i would allow three. Conversely, if a slave man marries a free woman, the Hanafi school allows three, while the Shafi'i allows only two.
Two scenarios are agreed upon: both free (three) and both slave (two).

Book of Divorce / Its Types and Conditions. There is no divorce of a minor, insane person, sleeping person, and a master over his slave's wife. And the divorce of a free woman and a slave woman is three and two [respectively]. And if their spouses are opposite of them [in status]. Indeed, the validity of divorce with us is based on women, and with al-Shafi'i based on men. So if the husband of a slave woman is free, then divorce with us is two, and with him three. And if the husband of a free woman is a slave, then divorce with us is three, and with him two. And a slave husband and his wife a slave woman: in it he owns two divorces, so she becomes irrevocable by two. These two scenarios are agreed upon. End. And supporting it is the practice of scholars according to it, as al-Tirmidhi mentioned. And also supporting it is that the dissolution of marital bond is a blessing and slavery has an effect in diminishing it. [1] His saying: 'based on women' like considering the waiting period, for the waiting period of a slave woman is half of that of a free woman, and it is made two menstrual cycles because of the impossibility of dividing a single menstrual cycle. [2] His saying: 'and with him three' so she does not become irrevocably separated after two divorces, but after the third.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Sharh al-Wiqayah, expanding on a matn from al-Wiqayah regarding divorce. The matn first states that divorce is invalid from a minor, insane person, sleeping person, and a master over his slave's wife. The commentary explains that a master does not own the marriage of his slave's wife; only the slave owns it, and cites a hadith: 'Divorce is from the one who takes the shank' (an expression for the husband). The matn then states that a free woman has three divorces and a slave woman has two, and adds 'and if their spouses are opposite of them'—meaning if the husband of a free woman is a slave or vice versa. The commentary clarifies that this creates four scenarios, and then delves into the inter-madhhab dispute: According to the Hanafi school, the number of divorces is based on the wife's status (free/slave), while according to Imam al-Shafi'i, it is based on the husband's status. For example, if a free man marries a slave woman, Hanafis say he may only issue two divorces (her capacity), while Shafi'is say he may issue three (his capacity). Conversely, if a slave man marries a free woman, Hanafis say he may issue three divorces (her capacity), while Shafi'is say only two (his capacity). Two scenarios are agreed upon: slave with slave (two divorces) and free with free (three). The text then provides the respective proofs. The Shafi'i proof is a mawquf hadith: 'Divorce is by men, and the waiting period by women' attributed to Ibn Abbas, Zayd ibn Thabit, and others. The Hanafi proof is a marfu' hadith: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles' reported by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi, though its chain is weak. However, Ibn al-Humam considered it hasan, and the commentary notes that scholars have acted upon it. The commentary also supports the Hanafi position by reasoning that slavery diminishes blessings, including the power of divorce. The passage ends with additional notes: the waiting period analogy and a Quranic verse supporting the counting of 'iddah.

Key Terms
صبي (sabi): A minor boy who has not reached puberty; his divorce is invalid because he lacks legal capacity (ahliyyah).
مجنون (majnun): An insane person, regardless of continuous or intermittent insanity; his divorce is invalid.
نائم (na'im): A sleeping person; his divorce is invalid due to lack of consciousness and intention.
سيد (sayyid): Master/owner of a slave; his divorce of his slave's wife is invalid because he does not own the marriage bond.
حرة (hurrah): A free woman; has three divorces available to her husband.
أمة (amah): A slave woman; has two divorces available to her husband.
طلاق (talaq): Divorce; dissolution of marriage by husband's pronouncement.

The number of divorces is determined by the wife's status in the Hanafi school, a position supported by a hadith and the practice of scholars.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
10 min
Number of Divorces: Wife's Status or Husband's?
Explaining Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on talaq counts based on wife's status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school bases the number of divorces on the wife's status and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The number of divorces depends on the wife's status, not the husband's — slavery diminishes the blessing of marriage.

This passage presents a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on a key issue in divorce law: does the number of permissible pronouncements of talaq depend on the status of the wife or the husband? While both schools agree on the basic rule that a free woman requires three divorces for irrevocable separation, they diverge when a slave wife is involved. Understanding this dispute requires careful examination of the textual evidence and rational principles that each school employs.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: the number of divorces that cause baynunah (irrevocable separation) depends on the wife's status. A slave wife becomes irrevocable after two divorces; a free wife after three.
This applies regardless of the husband's status.
2
Identify the opponent (Shafi'i) and their view
The Shafi'i school holds that the number depends on the husband's status: a slave husband can only give two divorces, a free husband three, irrespective of the wife's status.
This leads to opposite rulings in mixed-status marriages.
3
Analyse the Shafi'i proof
The Shafi'i rely on the hadith 'al-talaqu bi al-rijali wa al-iddatu bi al-nisa' (divorce is by men, waiting period by women), narrated as mawquf from several Companions. Evaluate its strength.
Mawquf means it is attributed to a Companion, not to the Prophet, making it less authoritative in Hanafi usul.
4
Analyse the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'talaqu al-amati thintan wa iddatuha haydatani' (divorce of a slave woman is two, her waiting period is two cycles). Though transmitted with a weak chain, Ibn al-Humam considers it hasan, and it is supported by scholarly practice and rational argument (istihsan).
The rational argument: marriage is a blessing, and slavery (riqq) diminishes rights, so the number of divorces is reduced.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference affects real cases: a free man married to a slave woman becomes irrevocable after two divorces in Hanafi law but after three in Shafi'i law. Conversely, a slave man married to a free woman becomes irrevocable after three in Hanafi but after two in Shafi'i.
Both schools agree on cases where both spouses have the same status (both free or both slave).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن اعتبار الطلاق عندنا بالنساء ، وعند الشَّافِعِي بالرجال ، فإذا كان زوج الأمة حراً، فالطلاق عندنا اثنان ، وعنده ثلاثة، وإن كان زوج الحرة عبداً ، فالطلاق عندنا ثلاثة ، وعنده اثنان. وزوج عبد وزوجته أمة ، وفيها يملك الطلاقين، فتبين باثنين، وهاتان الصورتان اتفاقيتان. وزوج حرّ وزوجته أمة ، وفيها تبين عندنا باثنين، وعند الشافعي له بثلاث. وزوج عبد وزوجته حرّة، وفيها تبين عندنا بثلاث، وعنده باثنين، له حديث : «الطلاق بالرجال، والعدة بالنساء، روي موقوفاً في «مصنف عبد الرزاق» على ابن عباس وزيد بن ثابت وعثمان وغيره . ولنا حديث: «طلاقُ الأمة ثنتان وعدتها حيضتان، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي بسند ضعيف مرفوعاً، وقال ابن الهمام بعد بسط ما له وما عليه : «إن لم يكن هذا الحديث صحيحاً كان حسناً». انتهى. ومما يؤيده عمل العلماء على وفقه، كما ذكره الترمذي، ويؤيده أيضاً أنّ حلّ المحلية نعمة والرق أثر في تنقصيها. [١] قوله : بالنساء ؛ كاعتبار العدّة ، فإنّ عدّة الأمة نصف عدة الحرّة، وإنما جعل حيضتين ؛ لعدم إمكان تجزئ الحيضة الواحدة، ويؤيده قوله تعالى : {إِذَا طَلَقْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ فَطَلِقُوهُنَّ لِعِدَّتِهِنَّ وَأَحْصُوا الْعِدَّةَ} [۲] قوله : وعنده ثلاثة ؛ فلا تبين بعد الطلاقين بينونة مغلظة، بل بعد الثالث.
Indeed, the consideration of divorce with us is based on women, and with al-Shafi'i is based on men. So if the husband of a slave woman is free, then divorce with us is two, and with him is three. And if the husband of a free woman is a slave, then divorce with us is three, and with him is two. And (the case of) a slave husband and his wife being a slave woman: in her he possesses two divorces, so she becomes irrevocable by two. These two cases are agreed upon. And (the case of) a free husband and his wife being a slave woman: she becomes irrevocable with us by two, and with al-Shafi'i he has three. And (the case of) a slave husband and his wife being a free woman: she becomes irrevocable with us by three, and with him by two. He (al-Shafi'i) has the hadith: 'Divorce is by the men, and the waiting period is by the women,' narrated as mawquf in the Musannaf of Abd al-Razzaq from Ibn Abbas, Zayd ibn Thabit, Uthman and others. And we have the hadith: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles,' narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi with a weak chain marfu'an, and Ibn al-Humam said after elaborating on its pros and cons: 'If this hadith is not authentic, it is at least hasan.' End. And what supports it is the practice of scholars in accordance with it, as al-Tirmidhi mentioned, and it is also supported by the fact that the permissibility of the marriage bond is a blessing, and slavery has an effect in diminishing it. [1] His statement: 'based on women' means like the consideration of the waiting period, for the waiting period of a slave woman is half that of a free woman, and it is made two menstrual cycles because a single cycle cannot be split. And it is supported by His statement, Most High: {When you divorce women, divorce them for their waiting period and count the waiting period} [2] His statement: 'and with him it is three' means she does not become irrevocably divorced after two divorces, but only after the third.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the number of divorces a husband can pronounce that cause irrevocable separation (baynunah). The Hanafi position, based on the text of the Qur'an and hadith, considers the wife's status (free or slave) as the determinant. For a slave wife, regardless of whether the husband is free or slave, the maximum is two divorces; after two, she becomes irrevocable. For a free wife, the maximum is three. The Shafi'i position, in contrast, considers the husband's status. The text presents four scenarios: (1) free husband with slave wife – Hanafi: 2, Shafi'i: 3; (2) slave husband with free wife – Hanafi: 3, Shafi'i: 2; (3) slave husband with slave wife – both agree on 2; (4) free husband with free wife – both agree on 3. The Hanafi proof includes the hadith 'The divorce of a slave woman is two...' which, though transmitted with a weak chain, is considered hasan by Ibn al-Humam and supported by scholarly practice. Additionally, rational argument (istihsan) is used: marriage is a blessing (ni'mah), and slavery diminishes rights, so the number of divorces is reduced as a consequence. The Shafi'i proof relies on a hadith attributed to Ibn Abbas and others, but it is mawquf (stopped at Companion level) and not marfu' (raised to the Prophet). Thus, the Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling.

Key Terms
الطلاق (al-talaq): The dissolution of the marriage bond by the husband's pronouncement, with the number of permissible pronouncements varying based on the wife's status.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death, before she can remarry. The passage mentions that a slave woman's iddah is two menstrual cycles, half that of a free woman.
الرق (al-riqq): Slavery; a legal status that reduces certain rights and obligations, such as the number of divorces and the length of the waiting period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many divorces cause irrevocable separation (baynunah) for a free woman married to a slave husband?
A) Two divorces
B) Three divorces
C) One divorce
D) The number depends on the husband's status

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the number of divorces that cause irrevocable separation depends on the wife's status: two for a slave woman, three for a free woman, regardless of the husband's status.

Sarih (Explicit) Talaq: Definition, Criteria, and Examples
Defining sarih talaq and its examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which words constitute sarih (explicit) talaq according to the Hanafi school and distinguish them from kinayah (implicit) talaq, applying the criterion of 'urf (custom).

Core Idea
A word is sarih if it is exclusively or predominantly used for divorce in the common language of the people, regardless of its linguistic origin.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah defines the crucial distinction between sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit) divorce words. Understanding this distinction is essential because sarih words effect divorce immediately without requiring the husband's intention, while kinayah words require proof of intent. The text provides the definition, the rationale based on usage and language, and several examples, including distorted words used by common people.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that the passage divides divorce words into sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit). The definition of sarih is given first.
2
Analyze the definition of sarih
Understand that sarih is defined as 'ما استعمل فيه دون غيره' (what is used for it and not for other things). This means a word exclusively or predominantly used for divorce.
The commentary expands: whether literal or figurative, as long as usage prevails.
3
Extract the criterion for sarih
Note that the criterion is not the linguistic root but actual custom ('urf). This is why distorted words like 'Tala' (for Talaq) can be sarih if commonly used only for divorce.
The text mentions that if one spells out the letters of talaq, it is not sarih but kinayah.
4
Review the examples provided
Go through the explicit examples: 'Anti taliq', 'Muttalaqa', 'Tallaqtuk' and additional ones from the commentary like 'Kuni taliqan', 'Utliqi', 'Ya muttalaqa', 'Ablaqtuk'. Understand that the list is not exhaustive.
Also note that the word must be spoken, but gestures and writing can substitute for the mute.
5
Apply the distinction
Given a scenario, classify the word as sarih or kinayah based on the criteria of exclusivity and custom, and state whether intention is required.

[٤]قوله : مثل؛ أشار به إلى أنه ليس الصريح منحصراً في هذا الألفاظ، فيدخل فيه : كوني طالقاً، وأطلقي، ويا مطلقة بتشديد اللام، وأطلقتك. كذا في «البحر»
Chapter of Effecting Divorce. Its explicit form: what is used for it and not for other things, such as: 'You are divorced' (Anti taliq), 'Divorced' (Muttalaqa), and 'I divorce you' (Tallaqtuk). [1] His statement: 'Chapter of Effecting Divorce' – the intent is not to explain this infinitive meaning but to explain the rulings attached to that by which divorce is effected. What is in this chapter is like the detail of what was in the previous chapter, for what preceded was mention of divorce itself and its primary divisions and the presentation of general principles, and what is in this chapter is an explanation of the rulings of their particulars. Its purpose is to explain the specific words of divorce and its division according to them. [2] His statement: 'Its explicit form...' etc. They divided divorce into two types: explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinaya). The explicit is the word that is not used except for it or is predominantly used for it, whether literal or figurative. The implicit is the opposite of that. This is mentioned in al-Fath and others. [3] His statement: 'what is used' – the referent of 'what' is the word, for the pillar (rukn) of divorce is uttering a word that indicates it, so it does not occur merely by resolve and intention. This is in al-Binaya. And what stands in its place is attached to it, such as understandable gesture and writing. In its absolute use, there is an indication that the specificity of the Arabic language is not considered; rather, it includes it and other languages such as Persian, Hindi, and others. And in the absolute use of 'usage' there is an indication that it is not required that the word be literally coined for it; rather, the consideration is based on usage. Upon this, they based the occurrence of divorce by corrupted words (al-alfaz al-musahhafa) that are not used except for it, even if they contain distortion by common people, such as: Tala', Talak, Talag, Talak, and the like. But if he spelled out the letters of divorce and said to his wife: 'Alif, Nun, Ta, T, Alif, Lam, Qaf' (spelling the word 'talaq'), then it is of the category of kinaya; divorce occurs by it with intention. This is in al-Dhakhira. [4] His statement: 'such as' – he indicated by it that the explicit is not confined to these words; rather, it includes: 'Be divorced' (Kuni taliqan), 'Be divorced' (Utliqi) – with emphasis on the lam, 'O divorced woman' (Ya muttalaqa), and 'I divorced you' (Ablaqtuk). This is in al-Bahr.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinaya) words of divorce. It begins by stating that the chapter's purpose is not to define the concept of effecting divorce but to explain the rulings related to the specific words used. The matn defines sarih as 'what is used for divorce and not for other things,' giving examples: 'Anti taliq' (You are divorced), 'Muttalaqa' (Divorced), and 'Tallaqtuk' (I divorce you). The commentary expands on this: scholars divided divorce words into two categories: sarih and kinaya. Sarih is a word that is exclusively or predominantly used for divorce, whether literal or figurative. Kinaya is other than that. The commentary on the phrase 'what is used' notes that the rukn (pillar) of talaq is verbal utterance; mere intention does not effect divorce. However, gestures (for the mute) and writing also suffice. The language is not restricted to Arabic; any language works. Importantly, the validity of sarih words does not depend on their linguistic origin (wad') but on common usage ('urf). Thus, even distorted words like 'Tala' (mispronunciation of 'Talaq') are effective if they are exclusively used for divorce. But if someone spells out the letters (e.g., 'Alif Nun Ta Alif Lam Qaf' – spelling 'talaq'), it is kinaya and requires intention. The examples given show that many wordings are included under sarih, as long as they are commonly understood to mean divorce. This ruling is based on the principle that divorce is a serious matter and the law gives effect to what is established in custom.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): A word that is used exclusively or predominantly for divorce, such that it effects divorce without the need for intention (niyyah).
كناية (kinayah): A word that is not exclusively used for divorce; it requires intention (niyyah) to effect divorce.
اللفظ المصحف (al-lafz al-musahhaf): A corrupted or mispronounced word that is commonly used by common people for divorce, e.g., 'Tala' instead of 'Talaq'.

Explicit Divorce (Ṣarīḥ) and Its Ruling
Defines explicit divorce and its ruling without intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of explicit divorce (ṣarīḥ) and apply its ruling that intention is not required for its validity, while also identifying the conditions under which it is effective.

Core Idea
Explicit divorce words effect divorce without intention—the word itself suffices, not the thought.

In this passage, the commentator explains a foundational distinction in Hanafi divorce law: explicit (ṣarīḥ) versus implicit (kināyah) words. The central ruling is that when a husband uses an explicit word of divorce, the divorce is effective as a single revocable divorce, regardless of whether he intended divorce, intended the opposite, or had no intention at all. However, this powerful effect is subject to several conditions, such as the husband knowing the meaning and consciously addressing his wife. This lesson will walk you through the definition, the ruling, and the conditions that safeguard against unintended divorces.

Guided Path
1
Define explicit divorce
Identify the Hanafi definition: a word used exclusively or predominantly for divorce, whether literal or figurative, e.g., 'You are divorced'.
Contrast with implicit words that require intention.
2
State the ruling without intention
Understand that explicit words effect one revocable divorce whether or not the husband intended divorce, intended its opposite, or had no intention.
The basis is the hadith of divorce in jest.
3
List the conditions for validity
Identify the necessary conditions: husband must utter a word indicating divorce, know its meaning, intend to address his wife, and not be under duress or a slip of the tongue.
Also includes misspelled words used commonly for divorce.
4
Recognize the cross-madhhab difference
Note that the Shafi'i school generally requires intention even for explicit words, making this a key practical difference.
This affects fatwa in diverse contexts.

Chapter on Effecting Divorce. Its explicit form: what is used in it and not in other [meanings], such as: 'You are divorced', 'divorced [woman]', and 'I divorced you'. He said: Chapter on Effecting Divorce; the intended meaning is not to explain this verbal-noun meaning, but rather to explain the rulings connected to that by which it is effected. So what is in this chapter is like a detail of what was in the previous chapter. For what preceded was mentioned of divorce itself and its primary divisions and presentation of general principles, and what is in this chapter is an explanation of the rulings of its particulars, and the purpose is to explain the specific words of divorce and its division with respect to them. He said: His explicit form... etc.; they divided divorce into two types: explicit and implicit. The explicit is the word that was not used except in it or is used mostly in it, whether literally or figuratively. And the implicit is the opposite, as mentioned in 'al-Fath' and others. He said: 'what is used'; the intended meaning of 'what' is the word, for the pillar of divorce is uttering a word that indicates it, so it does not occur by mere resolve and intention, as in 'al-Binaya'. And what stands in its place, like understood gesture and writing, is attached to it. And in its absolute [form] is an indication that specificity of the Arabic language is not considered, rather it includes it and other [languages] like Persian, Hindi, and others. And in the absolute [form] of usage is an indication that it is not conditional that it be a [lexically] assigned [term], but rather consideration is for usage, and upon this they based the occurrence of divorce by misspelled words that are not used except in it, even if they contain distortion by common people, like 'talaa', 'talak', 'talaq', 'talak' and the like. But if he spells the letters of divorce, saying to his wife: 'alif, noon, ta, alif, lam, qaf', it is from the category of implicit [words]; divorce occurs by it with intention, as in 'al-Dhakhira'. He said: 'such as'; he indicated by this that the explicit is not limited to these words, so it includes: 'Be divorced', 'Become divorced', 'O divorced [woman]' with doubled lam, and 'I divorced you', as in 'al-Bahr'. And by it occurs one revocable [divorce] even if he intended its opposite, or did not intend anything. And by it occurs one revocable [divorce], even if he intended its opposite: i.e., the opposite of the one revocable, which is the one irrevocable, or more than one. The wording of 'al-Mukhtasar': 'And by it occurs a revocable always' i.e., whether he did not intend, or intended one revocable, or irrevocable, or more than one, or did not intend anything. And in all [cases] it is necessary to add it to his wife by way of address or otherwise, whether it is explicit or understood by context. He said: 'one'; an adjective for a deleted [noun], i.e., one revocable divorce if there is nothing that makes it irrevocable. So the explicit is of two types: a type by which revocable divorce occurs, with which he has the right to take her back during the waiting period, and a type by which irrevocable divorce occurs, which requires a new marriage contract before or after tahlil. He said: 'i.e., opposite... etc.'; an indication that the pronoun refers to the one revocable, not to 'one' only, nor to 'revocable'. The upshot is that the occurrence of divorce by explicit words does not need intention, rather it occurs by them, whether he intended something or did not intend anything, and whether he intended it or intended its opposite. The basis for this is the hadith of the occurrence of divorce with jest, as we mentioned. Yes, for its occurrence judicially and religiously, it is necessary that he intend to attribute the word of divorce to her, knowing its meaning, and not diverting it to what it might imply. So if he repeated divorce cases in the presence of his wife addressing her, or wrote a quotation: 'My wife is divorced' with, or he was prompted to the word of divorce and he spoke it not knowing its meaning, it does not occur either judicially or religiously. And if he intended to speak words and his tongue slipped to 'You are divorced', she is not divorced religiously, as in 'al-Fath' and 'al-Nahr'. He said: 'did not intend anything'; as for if he intended by divorce [the meaning of] release from a tie or fetter, he is believed religiously if he did not accompany it with a number, and if he was coerced, he is believed judicially as well, as in 'al-Bahr'.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the chapter of effecting divorce. The author begins by clarifying that the chapter aims to explain the rulings of the words by which divorce is effected, not the linguistic meaning of the term 'iqa'. He then defines the two types of divorce words: explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinayah). Explicit words are those used only for divorce or predominantly for it, whether literal or figurative. Implicit words are the opposite. The author emphasizes that the pillar of divorce is the utterance of a word indicating it; mere intention is insufficient. However, explicit words do not require intention for the divorce to take effect legally and religiously—they effect one revocable divorce regardless of the husband's intention, even if he intended the opposite or no intention. The basis is the hadith that divorce occurs even in jest. Nonetheless, the husband must consciously direct the word to his wife and understand its meaning; if he repeats cases of divorce without intending to divorce, or his tongue slips, or he is prompted and does not understand, the divorce does not occur. Misspelled words commonly used among the laity for divorce (like 'talaa' instead of 'talaq') also count as explicit, but spelling out the letters is implicit, requiring intention. The passage clarifies that explicit divorce words are not limited to the three mentioned; other phrases like 'be divorced' etc. also qualify. The ruling is that the divorce is one revocable divorce during the waiting period, unless there is something making it irrevocable (e.g., being the third divorce).

Key Terms
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit word of divorce that is used only for divorce or predominantly for it, whether literal or figurative.
كناية (kināyah): Implicit word that can be used for divorce but is also used for other meanings, requiring intention for divorce to take effect.
رجعي (rijʿī): Revocable divorce during the waiting period, where the husband can take back his wife without a new marriage contract.
بائن (bāʾin): Irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately; the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage contract and her consent (and possibly tahlil if it is a third divorce).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'أنت طالق' (You are divorced) without any intention to divorce, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) It does not count as divorce.
B) It counts as one revocable divorce.
C) It counts as one irrevocable divorce.
D) It counts as three divorces.

Key Takeaway
Explicit divorce words (ṣarīḥ) effect a valid revocable divorce without need for intention, provided the husband knows the meaning and addresses his wife consciously.

Explicit Divorce Words: One Revocable Divorce Without Intention
Ruling with proof: explicit divorce words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that explicit divorce words effect one revocable divorce without need for intention and identify the conditions that affect its validity.

Core Idea
Explicit divorce words require no intention—they always effect one revocable divorce.

In the Hanafi school, the power of explicit divorce words (alfaz al-sariha) is absolute: they trigger a revocable divorce regardless of what the husband intends. This lesson unpacks that rule, its proof from the hadith of jest divorce, and the necessary conditions for it to take effect judicially and religiously.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
From the passage, determine the legal consequence of uttering explicit divorce words such as 'anti taliq'. The ruling is that it effects one revocable divorce (talq raj'i) even if the husband intends otherwise or has no intention.
The ruling applies to all explicit words unless there is something that makes it irrevocable (e.g., third divorce).
2
Understand the role of intention
The passage explicitly states that intention does not affect the occurrence of divorce with explicit words. Whether the husband intends a different type of divorce or no divorce at all, the same result—one revocable divorce—occurs.
This distinguishes explicit words from kinayah (allusive) words, where intention is required.
3
Examine the proof
The author cites the hadith that divorce in jest is valid and effective. This hadith serves as the proof (dalil) that intention is irrelevant for the occurrence of divorce—the mere utterance suffices.
The hadith is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut) but is accepted in the Hanafi school.
4
List the conditions for effectiveness
Although intention is not required for the divorce to occur, the passage mentions necessary conditions: the husband must address the wife (or intend to address her), he must know the meaning of the word, and he must not be using the word in a different sense (e.g., 'release from a rope').
These conditions apply both judicially (qada'an) and religiously (diyanatan), with some nuances.
5
Apply to edge cases
Consider scenarios like tongue slip, coercion, or literal interpretation. The passage explains that if the husband's tongue slips, the divorce is not valid religiously. If he is coerced, it is not valid even judicially. If he intends the literal meaning of 'release', he is believed religiously (if no number is mentioned).
These exceptions show that the ruling is not absolute; certain external factors can prevent the divorce from taking effect.

And it falls as one revocable [divorce], even if he intends its opposite, or did not intend anything. And it falls as one revocable [divorce] even if he intends its opposite: meaning the opposite of the one revocable, which is the one irrevocable, or more than one. And the wording of al-Mukhtasar: 'And it falls as revocable forever': meaning whether he did not intend, or intended one revocable, or irrevocable, or more than one, or did not intend anything. And in all cases, it is necessary to add it to his wife by way of address or otherwise, whether explicit or indicated by context. He said: 'one': an attribute of a deleted noun, i.e., one revocable divorce if there is nothing that makes it irrevocable. So the explicit [words] are of two types: a type by which revocable divorce occurs, in which he has the right to take her back during the waiting period, and a type by which irrevocable divorce occurs, which requires a new marriage before or after tahlil. He said: 'i.e., against... etc.': an indication that the pronoun refers to the one revocable, not to the one only, nor to the revocable. The gist is that the occurrence of divorce by explicit words does not require intention; rather it occurs by them, whether he intended something or did not intend anything, and whether he intended it or intended otherwise. The basis for this is the hadith of the occurrence of divorce in jest, as we mentioned. Yes, for its occurrence judicially and religiously, it is necessary to intend the addition of the word of divorce to her while knowing its meaning, and not diverting it to what it could bear. So if he repeatedly recited the issues of divorce in the presence of his wife addressing [her], or wrote 'my wife is divorced' in dictation, or he was prompted with the word of divorce and he spoke it without knowing its meaning, it does not occur judicially nor religiously. And if he intended to speak something and his tongue preceded to 'you are divorced', she is not divorced religiously. Thus in al-Fath and al-Nahr. He said: 'did not intend anything': as for if he intended by divorce the release from a binding or a fetter, he is believed religiously if he did not accompany it with a number, and if he was coerced, he is believed judicially as well. Thus in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining the general rule for explicit divorce words (alfaz al-sariha) in the Hanafi school. The author states that such words always effect a single revocable divorce (talq raj'i), regardless of the husband's intention—whether he intends a different type (e.g., irrevocable) or more than one divorce, or has no intention at all. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) from the Zahir al-Riwayah. The passage then clarifies that there are two categories of explicit words: those that cause revocable divorce (where the husband can take his wife back during the iddah) and those that cause irrevocable divorce (ba'in, requiring a new marriage). The key principle is that explicit words do not require niyyah (intention) for their effect; they take effect simply by utterance, as established by the hadith that even a divorce said in jest is valid. However, the author adds necessary conditions for the divorce to be effective both judicially (qada'an) and religiously (diyanatan): the husband must intend to address his wife with the word 'talaq' knowing its meaning, and not be using it in a different sense (e.g., releasing a rope). Examples are given: if a man repeats divorce cases as a lesson or is prompted to say 'taliq' without understanding, the divorce does not occur; if his tongue slips while trying to say something else, it is not valid religiously though it may be valid judicially. The passage also mentions that if a man intends by 'talaq' the literal meaning of 'release' (from a fetter), he is believed religiously as long as no number accompanies the word, and if coerced, he is believed judicially as well.

Key Terms
الصريح (al-sarih): Explicit divorce words: terms clearly understood as divorce, such as 'taliq' and its derivatives, without need for context or intention.
الرجعي (al-raj'i): Revocable divorce: a divorce in which the husband may take his wife back during the waiting period (iddah) without a new marriage contract.
البائن (al-ba'in): Irrevocable divorce: a divorce that severs the marriage tie completely, requiring a new marriage contract (and, for a third time, tahlil) to reunite.

Explicit divorce words automatically effect one revocable divorce; intention only becomes relevant in specific exceptions like literal interpretation or coercion, and then only religiously or judicially.

Intention in Divorce: The Infinitive vs. the Derivative
Rulings on divorce phrases like 'ant al-talaq' and the role of intention in determining the number of divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce using the infinitive, explain why intention of three is effective but intention of two is not, and evaluate Zufar's counter-argument using the concept of tawahhud.

Core Idea
With the infinitive, intention of three divorces is effective; intention of two is not—because the singular word indicates oneness, not a specific number.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explores a subtle distinction in Hanafi divorce law: when a husband uses a phrase like 'You are the divorce' (using the infinitive), his intention matters differently than when he uses a derivative like 'You are divorced'. The text establishes that the infinitive allows intention of three divorces to take effect, but intention of two is ineffective. This leads to an intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar, who argues for the validity of intending two. By working through this passage, you will grasp the Hanafi principle that singular words do not denote pure numbers, and you will see how this principle shapes the law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrases
Distinguish between phrases using the infinitive (masdar) like 'ant al-talaq' and phrases using only the derivative (mushtaqq) like 'ant taliq'. The passage notes that the ruling differs between these two categories.
The presence of the infinitive (definite or indefinite) changes the legal effect of intention.
2
State the default ruling
For phrases with the infinitive, if the husband has no specific intention or intends one or two divorces, only one revocable divorce falls. This is the baseline.
For derivatives, intention of three is disregarded; here it is effective.
3
Recognize the exception for intention of three
If the husband intends three divorces when using the infinitive, three divorces occur. This is the key difference from the derivative form.
The infinitive has a capacity to bear multiple numbers, but only three is given effect via intention.
4
Understand Zufar's objection
Zufar argues: if intention of three is valid, then intention of two should also be valid because two is part of three and the infinitive can bear any number.
This is a logical argument from part-whole relationship.
5
Trace the Hanafi rebuttal
The majority responds that the singular word (al-mufrad) does not indicate a pure number; it indicates oneness (tawahhud) in essence, type, or genus. Therefore, it cannot convey the specific number two. Intention of three is an exception because three has a distinct legal significance (the third divorce makes the woman forbidden).
The concept of tawahhud is central: the word 'divorce' in singular form is not a numeral; it only suggests unity, not a count.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي أنت الطلاق، أو أنت طالق الطِّلاقُ، أو أنت طالق طلاقاً يقعُ واحدة رجعية إن لم ينو شيئاً، أو نوى واحدة أو اثنتين، وإن نوى ثلاثاً فثلاث، هذا في الحرّة، أما في الأمة فثنتان بمنزلة الثلاث في الحرة. قوله: وفي أنت الطلاق ... الخ؛ هذه الصور التي يخبر عنها بالمصدر معرفاً أو منكراً، أو باسم فاعل بعده مصدر معرفاً أو منكراً حكمها مغاير من وجه المصدر التي يخبرُ عنها بالمشتق فقط على الصور التي لا يذكر فيها المصدر، تقع فيها الطلاق الواحد مطلقاً. وفي هذه الصور تقعُ الواحد الرجعي إذا لم ينو شيئاً أو نوى الواحد رجعياً كان أو بائناً، أو نوى اثنتين، وأما إذا نوى فيها الثلاث يقع فيها الثلاث بخلاف الصور الأولى، فإنّ نية الثلاث هناك ملغاة. قوله: أو نوى واحدة أو اثنتين؛ أي بالمصدر، وأما لو نوى بالمشتق واحدةً وبالمصدر أخرى وقعت رجعيتان على رأي بعض المشايخ، وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف لها. ووجهه أن كلا منهما صالح لإيقاع الطلاقِ به، فصار كأنه قال: أنت طالق طالق، وعند فخر الإسلام لا تقع إلا واحدة؛ لأنَّ الطالق نعت، والطلاق مصدره جيء به للتأكيد، وهو الذي رجحه في «الفتح» و «النهر» وغيرهما. قوله: أو اثنتين؛ فيه خلافُ زفر، فإنّه يقول: الثنتان بعض الثلاث، فلمّا صحت نية الثلاث صحت نية الثنتين؛ لأنَّ المصدر يحتمل الواحد والاثنين والثلاث. ونحن نقول: إن معنى التوحد مراعاة في ألفاظ الواحدان إما باعتبار الذات، كزيد، وإما باعتبار النوع كرجل، وإما باعتبار الجنس كحيوان، ولا يدل لفظ المفردِ على العدد المحض، ومراعاة التوحد تكون بأحد أمرين، إما بالفردية بحسب الحقيقة، أو
And in [the phrases] 'You are the divorce', or 'You are divorced, the divorce', or 'You are divorced with a divorce', it falls as one revocable divorce if he did not intend anything, or intended one or two, and if he intended three, then three. This is for a free woman; as for a slave woman, then two are in the position of three for a free woman. He said: 'And in 'You are the divorce'... etc.': These forms that are reported using the infinitive (masdar) definite or indefinite, or with the active participle followed by an infinitive definite or indefinite, their ruling is different in one respect from the forms that are reported using only the derivative (mushtaqq) in the forms where the infinitive is not mentioned; in those, one divorce falls absolutely. And in these forms, one revocable divorce falls if he did not intend anything, or intended one whether revocable or irrevocable, or intended two. But if he intended three therein, three falls, unlike the first forms, where the intention of three is disregarded. He said: 'or intended one or two': i.e., by the infinitive. And as for if he intended one by the derivative and another by the infinitive, two revocable divorces fall according to the opinion of some shaykhs, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf. Its justification is that each of them is suitable for effecting divorce, so it is as if he said: 'You are divorced, divorced.' And according to Fakhr al-Islam, only one falls because 'taliq' is a noun attribute and 'talaq' is its infinitive brought for emphasis. This is what was preferred in al-Fath and al-Nahr and others. He said: 'or two': there is a difference of opinion from Zufar, for he says: two is part of three, so since the intention of three is valid, the intention of two is valid because the infinitive can bear one, two, or three. And we say: the meaning of oneness (tawahhud) is observed in the words of the 'one' either with respect to essence, like Zayd, or with respect to kind, like 'a man', or with respect to genus, like 'animal'. And the singular word does not indicate pure number. The observance of oneness is achieved by one of two matters: either by individuation according to reality, or...
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of intention in divorce phrases that include an infinitive (masdar). It begins with the ruling: when a husband says 'ant al-talaq' (you are the divorce) or similar phrases, the default is one revocable divorce if no specific intention, or if he intends one or two divorces. However, if he intends three, then three divorces occur. This is contrasted with phrases using only the derivative (mushtaqq) like 'ant taliq', where intention of three is ineffective; only one divorce occurs. The passage then clarifies that if the husband intends one divorce by the derivative (e.g., 'ant taliq') and another by the infinitive (e.g., 'al-talaq'), some shaykhs, including a narration from Abu Yusuf, hold that two revocable divorces occur because each word is capable of effecting divorce. The more authoritative view (according to al-Fath and al-Nahr) is that only one occurs, as the infinitive is merely an emphasis. Finally, there is a dispute with Zufar regarding the intention of two: Zufar argues that since the intention of three is valid, intention of two should also be valid because the infinitive can bear any number. The Hanafi response is that the singular word (al-mufrad) does not indicate a pure number; rather, it indicates oneness in essence, type, or genus, not the specific number one. Thus, the intention of two cannot be derived from the singular form; it requires explicit number. The text cuts off before completing the discussion.

Key Terms
المصدر (al-masdar): The infinitive form of the verb, e.g., 'al-talaq' (the divorce) or 'talaqan' (a divorce), used in divorce phrases.
المشتق (al-mushtaqq): The derivative form, specifically the active participle 'taliq' (divorced), used in explicit divorce phrases.
التوحد (al-tawahhud): The concept of oneness or individuality as implied by singular words; distinct from the numeral 'one'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

In Hanafi law, when a divorce is pronounced using the infinitive, intention of three divorces is effective, but intention of two is not—because the singular word indicates oneness, not a specific number.

Divorce by Reference to Whole Body vs. Limbs: The Criterion of Custom
Ruling on adding talaq to whole-denoting expressions vs. limbs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between expressions that automatically trigger talaq (those customarily denoting the whole) and those that do not, applying the Hanafi criterion of custom (urf).

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, talaq occurs automatically only when the husband uses an expression customarily understood to refer to the entire person, not a mere limb.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a key principle in Hanafi divorce law: the use of body parts as euphemisms for divorce. The text distinguishes between words that, by custom, represent the whole person—like head, neck, face—and those that do not—like hand, foot, back, belly. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying talaq rulings and avoiding unintended consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify expressions that trigger talaq automatically
List the words mentioned in the text that, when used in a divorce statement, automatically cause one talaq. These are words customarily used to refer to the whole person.
Examples: head (ra's), neck (raqaba), spirit (ruh), face (wajh), genitalia (farj).
2
Identify expressions that do NOT trigger talaq
List the words that do not trigger automatic talaq because they are not customarily used for the whole. These include hand, foot, back, belly, and similar limbs.
The text also mentions hair, nose, anus, shin, etc.
3
Understand the rationale: custom (urf) is decisive
Explain why 'hand' does not work even though it is used metaphorically in the Quran. The criterion is widespread custom, not occasional metaphorical usage.
The commentator explicitly addresses the objection using Quranic evidence.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding back and belly
Note that some early Hanafis considered 'back and belly' effective, but the preferred (azhar) view is that they are not. Know the mufta bihi position.
This affects fatwa practice.
5
Apply the ruling to new scenarios
Given a new expression (e.g., 'your heart is divorced'), determine if it is customarily used for the whole. If not, talaq occurs only if the husband intended the whole figuratively.
Use the principle: 'Ibrah fi hadha al-bab al-urf wa al-ishtihar.'

[٢] قوله : وإلى يدها ... الخ ؛ يعني لا يقع الطلاق بإضافته إلى ما لا يعبر به عن الكل عرفاً مشتهراً كاليد، والرجل، والظهر والبطن والشعر، والأنف، والدبر، والساق، واللسان، والأذن، والفم والصدر، والذقن، والسن، والريق، ونحو ذلك. فإن قلت : قد يطلق اليد وتراد به الذات، كما قال الله جلال: بِمَا قَدَّمَتْ يَدَاكَ فينبغي أن يقع بإضافته إليها. قلت : العبرة في هذا الباب العرفُ والاشتهار لا لمجرد وقوع الاستعمال ، فكل لفظ لم يشتهر إطلاقه على الذات لم يقع به ، نعم لو نوى به الذات مجازاً وقع ، بخلافِ ما اشتهر استعماله في الكل، فإنّه لا يحتاجُ هناك إلى نيّة الكل ؛ ولذا لا يصدق هناك قضاء إذا قال : أردت ذلك العضو بعينه. كذا في «فتح القدير وغيره.
And by adding divorce to all of her, or to what expresses the whole, such as 'you are divorced', or 'your head', 'your neck', 'your throat', 'your spirit', 'your body', 'your substance', 'your face', 'your genitalia', or to an undivided part like half of you or a third of you, it occurs; and to her hand or foot, no; likewise the back and belly, and this is the more apparent. [1] His statement: 'to what expresses the whole' means a limb that is customarily and conventionally used to express the essence, like the neck, as indicated by His statement: 'and the freeing of a believing neck' and 'if We will, We send down upon them from the sky a sign, so their necks remain humbled.' Likewise the throat, Allah said: 'so their necks remain humbled,' meaning their selves. Likewise the head: it is said 'so-and-so is the head of the people' and 'so-and-so freed such-and-such heads.' Likewise the spirit: it is said 'his spirit perished,' i.e., his self. Likewise the face: Allah said: 'so wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah,' i.e., His essence. Likewise the genitalia: the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Allah curses the genitalia upon saddles,' mentioned by the author of al-Hidayah, but the compilers of his hadith did not find it with this wording; rather with the wording: 'He forbade that those with genitalia should ride on saddles,' narrated by Ibn Adi in al-Kamil. [2] His statement: 'and to her hand... etc.' means that divorce does not occur by adding it to what is not conventionally and commonly used to express the whole, such as the hand, foot, back, belly, hair, nose, anus, shin, tongue, ear, mouth, chest, chin, tooth, saliva, and the like. Then if you say: 'The hand is sometimes used to refer to the self, as Allah Almighty said: 'by what your hands have sent forth,' so it should be that divorce occurs by adding it to it.' I say: 'The criterion in this chapter is custom and common usage, not mere occurrence of usage. So any word that is not commonly used to refer to the self does not effect divorce by it. Yes, if he intends the self figuratively, it occurs, unlike what is commonly used for the whole, for there is no need there for intention of the whole; hence he is not believed judicially if he says 'I meant that specific limb.' This is in Fath al-Qadir and others.'
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on the effect of adding divorce (talaq) to various expressions that refer to the wife's body. The matn states that if the husband says 'you are divorced' or adds divorce to a part that customarily expresses the whole person—such as head, neck, spirit, body, face, or genitalia—the talaq occurs. It also occurs if he adds it to an undivided fraction like half or one-third. However, if he adds it to a specific limb that is not customarily used for the whole, like hand or foot, the talaq does not occur. The same applies to the back and belly, which is the more apparent view. The commentary (sharh) first explains the term 'what expresses the whole' as a limb conventionally used to represent the essence, providing Quranic and linguistic evidence: the word 'neck' (raqaba) is used for a slave (e.g., taḥrīr raqaba), 'head' (ra's) for a person (ra's al-qawm), 'spirit' (rūḥ) for self (halaka rūḥuhu), 'face' (wajh) for essence (wajh Allāh). Even 'genitalia' (farj) is supported by a hadith, though the wording is disputed. The commentary then clarifies that limbs like hand, foot, etc., do not cause divorce because they are not commonly used to refer to the whole. It addresses an objection: since the Quran uses 'yad' (hand) to mean the self (e.g., 'bimā qaddamat yadāka'), why is it not effective? The response is that the criterion is widespread custom (al-'urf wa al-ishtihār), not occasional metaphorical use. If the husband intends the whole self figuratively, divorce occurs, but it is not automatic; the judge does not accept his claim of intending a specific limb. This ruling highlights the Hanafi reliance on 'urf as a source of law, especially in matters of oaths and divorce.

Key Terms
ما يعبر به عن الكل (mā yu'abbiru bihi 'an al-kull): An expression that is customarily used to refer to the entire person, such as 'head', 'neck', 'face', etc.
عرف (urf): Customary usage among people; here it is the determinant for whether a body part expression automatically triggers divorce.
الظهر والبطن (al-zahr wa al-baṭn): The back and belly; in this context, they are limbs that do not customarily denote the whole, so talaq does not occur without intent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife, 'Your hand is divorced,' without any additional intention, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) One talaq occurs because the hand is part of the body.
B) No talaq occurs because the hand is not customarily used to refer to the whole person.
C) One talaq occurs if the husband intended the whole wife.
D) No talaq occurs unless the wife files for divorce.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi talaq, the automatic effect of expressions relies on widespread custom (urf), not literal or occasional metaphorical usage.

Divorce by Fractions and Numerical Phrases
Ruling on fractional and numerical talaq expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that talaq is indivisible in occurrence to fractional utterances and interpret numerical phrases (min…ilā) to determine the number of divorces.

Core Idea
Half a talaq triggers a full talaq; 'one to two' gives one, 'one to three' gives two.

What happens when a husband says 'you are divorced by half a divorce' or 'from one to two'? The Hanafi school treats talaq as indivisible — you cannot split it. For numerical ranges, Abu Hanifa reads the difference between the two numbers. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and contrasts it with Zufar's alternative interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression type
Determine whether the husband uttered a fraction (e.g., half, third) or a numerical range (e.g., from one to two).
Fractions and ranges are treated differently in detail but share the principle of giving effect to speech.
2
Apply the principle of indivisibility
For fractions, the act of talaq (occurrence) cannot be divided. Uttering a part results in one complete talaq to avoid nullifying the speaker's words.
Hanafi: 'al-talaq lā yaqbalu al-tajazzu'a wuqū'an'.
3
Interpret numerical phrases
For 'from X to Y', Abu Hanifa takes the difference (Y minus X). 'One to two' gives 1; 'one to three' gives 2. The endpoints are not counted as additional divorces.
Zufar argues that both initial and final limits are excluded, so 'one to two' yields nothing, 'one to three' yields one.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Zufar differs: 'one to two' → no talaq (ambiguous), 'one to three' → one talaq (only middle numbers count). Abu Hanifa's view is the mufta bihi.
'Hādhā 'indahu' refers to Abu Hanifa; Zufar's view is not relied upon for fatwa.
5
State the operative ruling (mufta bihi)
Half/third → one talaq. 'From one to two' → one talaq. 'From one to three' → two talaq. This is the position of Abu Hanifa adopted by the majority of Hanafi scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢] قوله : يقع ؛ لأنه قد يطلقُ الظهر والبطن ويراد به الذات ، يقال : فلان يقوّي ظهرك. والجواب : أن صحة استعماله في الذاتِ لا يوجبُ وقوع الطلاق به بلا نية ، فإنّ مداره على اشتهاره عرفاً، وإذ ليس فليس وبالجملة: لا نزاع في الوقوع بهذه الألفاظ عند إرادة الذات مجازاً إنما النزاع عند عدم النية. [٣] قوله : وبنصف ؛ يعني إذا قال : أنت طالق نصف تطليقة أو ثلثها أو ربعها وغير ذلك من أجزائها يقعُ طلاق واحد ؛ لأن الطلاق لا يقبل التجزؤ وقوعاً، فَلَمَّا أوقع جزءاً من أجزائه تكامل ووقع الكلّ حذراً من إلغاء كلام العاقل البالغ. [٤] قوله : أو من واحدة ؛ يعني إذا قال : أنت طالق من واحدة إلى ثنتين، أو قال : أنت طالق ما بين واحدة إلى اثنتين يقعُ واحد، ولو قال : من واحدة إلى ثلاث أو ما بينَ واحدة إلى ثلث تقع ثنتان، هذا عنده. وعند زفر له في المسألة الأولى لا يقعُ شيء، ويقع في الثانية واحدة ؛ لعدم دخول الغايتين الابتدائية والانتهائية.
And by half a divorce or a third of it, or from one to two or between one and two — one, because they do not express the whole, and with some it occurs. [1] His statement: 'and by half a divorce or a third of it, or from one to two or between one and two — one' so his statement 'one' is the subject and its predicate is 'by half a divorce.' [2] His statement: 'it occurs' because the back and belly are sometimes used to refer to the self, it is said: 'So-and-so strengthens your back.' The answer is that the correctness of its usage for the self does not necessitate the occurrence of divorce by it without intention, for the criterion is its being commonly known customarily, and since it is not, it is not. In summary, there is no dispute about the occurrence with these expressions when intending the self figuratively; the dispute is only when there is no intention. [3] His statement: 'and by half' means if he says: 'You are divorced by half a divorce' or a third or a quarter, etc., one divorce occurs because divorce cannot be divided in occurrence; so when he performs a part of its parts, it is completed and the whole occurs to avoid nullifying the speech of a sane adult. [4] His statement: 'or from one' means if he says: 'You are divorced from one to two' or 'between one and two', one occurs; if he says 'from one to three' or 'between one and three', two occur. This is according to him (Abu Hanifa). And according to Zufar, in the first case nothing occurs, and in the second case one occurs because of the non-inclusion of the two limits, the beginning and the ending.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on talaq expressions, now focusing on fractional and numerical phrases. The matn states that if a husband says, 'You are divorced by half a divorce' or a third, or 'from one to two' or 'between one and two', only one divorce occurs. The commentary explains that the word 'one' is the predicate of 'half a divorce' etc. Then it seems there is a repetition of the earlier discussion about back and belly (possibly a scribal error), but the intended point is that divorce does not occur by mere mention of those limbs without intention. The main ruling: uttering a fraction of a divorce, like half or third, results in a full single divorce because talaq (the act of divorce) is indivisible in its occurrence (lā yaqbalu al-tajazzu'a wuqū'an). The husband's words are taken seriously; to avoid nullifying his speech, the divorce is deemed to have occurred completely. Similarly, the phrase 'from one to two' means one talaq (the lower number), but 'from one to three' means two talaq (the difference). The commentary notes a difference with Zufar (a Hanafi scholar): in the first case ('from one to two') Zufar says nothing occurs because the phrase is ambiguous and does not specify a clear number; in the second case ('from one to three') Zufar says one occurs because the effective range is between one and three, but three is not included as an end limit. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) is that the husband's intent is to give the difference between the numbers, so from one to two gives one (difference =1), and from one to three gives two (difference =2). This illustrates the Hanafi principle of giving effect to the speaker's words as much as possible when the meaning is clear.

Key Terms
تجزؤ (tajazzu'): Divisibility; here it means that the occurrence of talaq cannot be split into fractions; an utterance of half a divorce results in one full divorce.
غايتان ابتدائية وانتهائية (ghāyatān ibtidā'iyya wa intihā'iyya): The initial and final limits in a numerical phrase; Zufar argues that both limits must be excluded for the middle numbers to count.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says to his wife, 'You are divorced from one to three,' how many divorces occur?
A) One divorce, because the lower number is taken as the starting point.
B) Two divorces, because the difference between one and three is two.
C) Three divorces, because the upper limit is included.
D) No divorce, because the phrase is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, talaq is indivisible, so a fraction triggers a full talaq; for numerical phrases, Abu Hanifa's method of taking the difference is the operative ruling.
