Fractional Divorce Completes to One Full Divorce
Ruling with rational proof on fractional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fractional divorce expressions and explain the rationale that divorce is indivisible in occurrence.

Core Idea
Any fraction of a divorce utterance completes as one full divorce because divorce cannot be partially executed.

In this lesson, we examine a short but powerful rule: saying 'half a divorce' is not merely half — it is a complete divorce. The Hanafi school uniquely holds that the act of talaq does not tolerate fragmentation. When a sane adult utters any fraction, the entire divorce falls. This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the nature of the legal act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Fractional Expression
Recognize that the husband's words include a fraction of a divorce, e.g., 'half', 'third', 'quarter', or any other part.
The expression must be a clear fraction of the divorce unit.
2
State the Ruling
The Hanafi ruling is that one complete divorce falls — not a fraction, not nothing, but a full single talaq.
3
Understand the Rationale
The commentator says: 'because divorce does not accept division in occurrence'. The act of talaq is legally indivisible; if any part is uttered, the whole necessarily follows.
This is a rational (عقلي) proof, not based on a specific textual wording.
4
Apply the Principle
If a husband says 'you are divorced one-third', treat it as one full divorce. The fraction is disregarded except as an indication of intent to divorce at least once.
This applies regardless of the specific fraction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وبنصف؛ يعني إذا قال: أنت طالق نصف تطليقة أو ثلثها أو ربعها وغير ذلك من أجزائها يقع طلاق واحد؛ لأن الطلاق لا يقبل التجزؤ وقوعا، فلما أوقع جزءا من أجزائه تكامل وقع الكل حذرا من إلغاء كلام العاقل البالغ.
His saying: 'And with half' means that if he says: 'You are divorcing half a divorce' or a third of it or a quarter of it or other parts of it, one divorce falls, because divorce does not accept division in occurrence. So when he uttered a part of its parts, it completed and the whole fell, out of caution against nullifying the speech of a sane adult.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This is an expansion probe (sharh) explaining the matn's ruling on fractional divorce. The commentator clarifies that when a husband utters a fraction of a divorce (e.g., half, third, quarter), it results in one complete divorce. The rationale is that divorce is legally indivisible in its occurrence (وقوعا). Since divorce cannot be partially executed, the utterance of any part is treated as an expression of the whole, to avoid wasting the husband's speech. This is a unique Hanafi principle: the act of divorce is not susceptible to fragmentation, so the part stands for the whole. Note that this ruling applies only to the occurrence; the husband may still have a valid intention for fewer divorces if he specifies a number? Actually, the text says it falls as one regardless, so if he says half a divorce, it's one full divorce. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Key Terms
نصف تطليقة (nisf tatliqah): Half a divorce; a fractional expression of talaq that legally completes to a full divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Any fractional divorce expression results in one complete divorce because the act of talaq does not admit division.

Divorce via Body Parts: The Role of Customary Usage
Commentary on divorce via body part words and the role of custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between body part words that effect divorce without intention and those that require intention, applying the Hanafi principle of customary usage (urf).

Core Idea
Customary usage, not Quranic occurrence, decides which body part words imply the whole person in Hanafi divorce.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a critical nuance in Hanafi talaq law. While some body part words like 'back' are customarily understood to refer to the entire person, others like 'hand' are not. The ruling hinges on established custom (urf), not mere metaphorical use in Scripture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
The matn states that divorce falls when using body part words like 'back' because they are customarily used for the whole person. Note the assumption: such words refer to the entire self.
The phrase 'قوله: يقع' refers to the base text's ruling.
2
Examine the objection from Quranic usage
The commentator raises a potential objection: the Quran uses 'hand' to refer to the whole person (Quran 22:10). So why wouldn't saying 'Your hand is halal' also cause divorce?
This objection tests whether Quranic metaphorical usage suffices.
3
Understand the rebuttal using custom
The commentator replies that the criterion is not occasional usage but customary common usage (عرف واشتهار). The Quranic usage alone does not establish a general rule for divorce.
The key term: العبرة في هذا الباب العرف والاشتهار
4
Distinguish between customarily used words and others
Words like 'back' are customarily understood for the whole person; no additional intention needed. Words like 'hand' are not; divorce occurs only if the husband intends the whole person metaphorically.
Contrast: لا يحتاج هناك إلى نية الكل vs. يحتاج إلى نية
5
Apply the ruling
If a husband says 'Your back is forbidden' without intention, divorce falls. If he says 'Your hand is forbidden', divorce does not fall unless he intended the whole person.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: يقع؛ لأنه قد يطلق الظهر والبطن ويراد به الذات، يقال: فلان يقوي ظهرك.
والجواب: أن صحة استعماله في الذات لا يوجب وقوع الطلاق به بلا نية، فإن مداره على اشتهاره عرفا، وإذ ليس فليس. وبالجملة: لا نزاع في الوقوع بهذه الألفاظ عند إرادة الذات مجازا إنما النزاع عند عدم النية.
فإن قلت: قد يطلق اليد وتراد به الذات، كما قال الله تعالى: بِمَا قَدَّمَتْ يَدَاكَ فينبغي أن يقع بإضافته إليها. قلت: العبرة في هذا الباب العرف والاشتهار لا لمجرد وقوع الاستعمال، فكل لفظ لم يشتهر إطلاقه على الذات لم يقع به، نعم لو نوى به الذات مجازا وقع، بخلاف ما اشتهر استعماله في الكل، فإنه لا يحتاج هناك إلى نية الكل؛ ولذا لا يصدق هناك قضاء إذا قال: أردت ذلك العضو بعينه. كذا في «فتح القدير» وغيره.
ظهرك.
His saying: 'It falls' because the back and belly are sometimes used to refer to the whole person; one says: 'So-and-so strengthens your back.' The answer: The validity of its use for the whole person does not necessitate the occurrence of divorce without intention, because the criterion is its common usage customarily, and it is not so. In summary: There is no dispute that divorce occurs with these words when intending the whole person metaphorically; the dispute is only when there is no intention. If you say: The hand is sometimes used to refer to the whole person, as Allah Almighty said: 'What your hands have sent forth' so divorce should occur by adding it to her. I say: The criterion in this chapter is custom and common usage, not mere occurrence of usage. So every word that is not commonly used to refer to the whole person does not effect divorce. However, if one intends the whole person metaphorically, it does occur, contrary to that which is commonly used for the whole; there, there is no need for intention of the whole. Hence, it is not accepted judicially if he says: 'I meant that specific limb.' As in Fath al-Qadir and others. 'Your back.'
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe (sharh) on the matn's statement that divorce occurs when using certain body parts. The commentator first explains that words like 'back' or 'belly' can refer to the entire person in common parlance, so if a husband says 'Your back is forbidden to me,' a divorce falls. However, an objection arises: the Quran uses 'hand' to mean the whole person (e.g., 'what your hands have sent forth'), so why wouldn't saying 'Your hand is halal' also cause divorce? The answer is that the operative criterion is customary usage (عرف), not mere occasional Quranic usage. Only words conventionally understood to refer to the entire person cause divorce without additional intent. If a word like 'hand' is not customarily used for the whole, divorce does not fall unless the husband explicitly intends the whole person. Thus, the ruling is that for words not customarily used for the whole, a specific intention (نية) is required for divorce to occur. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الظهر (al-zahr): The back, used metaphorically for the whole person in some contexts.
اليد (al-yad): The hand, used in the Quran to refer to the whole person, but not customarily so in divorce context.
العرف (al-urf): Customary usage; the established common understanding in a community.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says 'Your hand is halal for me' without intending the whole person, does divorce occur?
A) Yes, because 'hand' can refer to the whole person in the Quran.
B) No, because 'hand' is not customarily used for the whole person in talaq.
C) Yes, if the wife intends it.
D) No, never.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi talaq, the decisive factor for body part words is customary usage, not Quranic precedent; intention is needed only for words not customarily used for the whole person.

Numeric Expressions in Talaq: Ranges and Fractions
Intra-Hanafi disputes on numeric talaq expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on numeric range and fraction expressions in talaq and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreements (Zufar, Sahibayn) and the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
In talaq, the first endpoint of a range is included but the last is not; fractions of divorces complete to the nearest whole.

When a husband uses numbers like 'from one to three' or 'three halves', the number of divorces that occur is not always obvious. The Hanafi school applies a restrictive principle (hazr) to favor the minimum, but scholars differ on how to interpret the endpoints. This lesson unpacks the rulings of Abu Hanifa, Zufar, and the Sahibayn, showing how the same phrase can yield different counts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrasing type
Determine whether the husband used a range (e.g., 'from one to three') or a fraction (e.g., 'three halves of two divorces').
Each type has its own rule.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
For a range, the first number is included, the last is excluded. For fractions, half of a set of divorces is interpreted as half of the total number.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Identify the disagreement
Note that Zufar excludes both endpoints; the Sahibayn include both. For fractions, some say three halves of two divorces equals two divorces.
These are valid ikhtilafat within the school.
4
Analyze the evidence
The principle of hazr (restriction) requires applying the minimum possible count. The first endpoint must exist to base the range on, but the last is not included because it would increase the number.
This is supported in al-Binayah.
5
Apply to a new expression
Practice: if a husband says 'you are divorcing from two to five', how many divorces occur according to the relied-upon view?
Answer: four (2,3,4; five excluded).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: أو من واحدة؛ يعني إذا قال: أنت طالق من واحدة إلى ثنتين، أو قال: أنت طالق ما بين واحدة إلى اثنتين يقع واحد. ولو قال: من واحدة إلى ثلاث أو ما بين واحدة إلى ثلاث تقع ثنتان، هذا عنده. وعند زفر له في المسألة الأولى لا يقع شيء، ويقع في الثانية واحدة؛ لعدم دخول الغايتين الابتدائية والانتهائية.
وفي: من واحدة إلى ثلاث أو ما بين واحدة إلى ثلاث اثنتان، وبثلاثة أنصاف طلقتين ثلاث، وبثلاثة أنصاف طلقة طلقتان، وقيل: ثلاث. وجه الأول: أن ثلاث أنصاف طلقة يكون طلقة ونصفا، فيتكامل النصف، فحصل طلقتان. وجه الثاني: أن كل نصف يتكامل فحصل ثلاث. وعند أبي يوسف ومحمد تدخل الغايتان، فتقع في الأولى ثنتان، وفي الثانية اثنان. وتوجيه قوله: أن الغاية الأولى لا بد أن تكون موجودة لترتب عليه الثانية، ووجودها بوقوعها، ولا يدخل الانتهاء؛ لأن الأصل في الطلاق الحظر، فيحكم بالأقل مهما أمكن. كذا في «البناية» وغيرها.
قوله: وبثلاثة؛ يعني إذا قال: أنت طالق ثلاثة أنصاف طلقتين، يقع ثلاث، وإن قال: ثلاثة أنصاف طلقة واحدة تقع اثنتان، وهذا هو الأصح في الصورتين، وقيل: في الصورة الأولى تقع ثنتان؛ لأن التطليقتين إذا نصفتا كانت أربعة أنصاف، فثلاثة منها يكون طلقة ونصفا، فيكمل النصف لعدم التجزؤ، ورد بأنه فرق بين نصف التطليقتين ونصف كل من التطليقتين، والموجب لأربعة أنصاف إنما هو المعنى الثاني، وهو خلاف الظاهر، نعم اللفظ يحتمله، فلو نواه صدق ديانة، والظاهر هو أن نصف التطليقتين تطليقة، فتقع ثلاث بثلاثة أنصاف تطليقتين. وقيل: في الصورة الثانية تقع ثلاث؛ لأن النصف لا بد من تكميله، فثلاثة أنصاف إذا كملت تكون ثلاث تطليقات كوامل.
His saying: 'Or from one' means that if he says: 'You are divorcing from one to two,' or says: 'between one and two,' one falls. And if he says: 'from one to three' or 'between one and three,' two fall, according to him (Abu Hanifa). According to Zufar, in the first case nothing falls, and in the second one falls, because the starting and ending points are not included. And in: 'from one to three or between one and three, two,' and with three halves of two divorces, three, and with three halves of one divorce, two, and it is said: three. The justification of the first: three halves of one divorce become one and a half, so the half completes, resulting in two. The justification of the second: every half completes, resulting in three. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, both endpoints are included, so in the first case two fall, and in the second two. And the justification of his saying: the first endpoint must exist for the second to be based on it, and its existence is by its occurrence, and the endpoint is not included because the principle in divorce is restriction (hazr), so the minimum is ruled whenever possible. As in al-Binayah and others. His saying: 'And with three' means if he says: 'You are divorcing three halves of two divorces,' three fall, and if he says: 'three halves of one divorce,' two fall, and this is the most correct in both cases. And it is said: in the first case two fall because two divorces when halved become four halves, so three of them are one and a half, so the half completes due to indivisibility. It is refuted by saying it distinguishes between half of two divorces and half of each of the two divorces, and the cause for four halves is the second meaning, which is contrary to the apparent. However, the wording can bear it, so if he intends it, it is truthful privately, but the apparent is that half of two divorces is one divorce, so three fall with three halves of two divorces. And it is said: in the second case three fall because the half must be completed, so three halves when completed become three complete divorces.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn's rulings for numeric expressions in talaq. It covers three sub-issues: (1) 'From one to two' results in one divorce according to Abu Hanifa, while Zufar says nothing falls because the endpoints are not included. (2) 'From one to three' results in two divorces according to Abu Hanifa; Zufar says one; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say two with both endpoints included. The commentator explains that Abu Hanifa's position is that the first endpoint (one) occurs, but the last (three) is not included because talaq is restrictive and the minimum is applied. (3) Expressions like 'three halves of two divorces' result in three divorces in the relied-upon view (though there are sayings for two), and 'three halves of one divorce' result in two. The reasoning involves the principle that divorce cannot be divided, so halves complete. The intra-Hanafi disputes are detailed: Zufar differs on the inclusion of endpoints; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on the inclusion of the final endpoint. The mufta bihi (operative) position is the one attributed to Abu Hanifa in the text, with the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) sometimes agreeing or differing. This shows the richness of Hanafi legal reasoning with multiple views.

Key Terms
الغاية الابتدائية (al-ghāyah al-ibtidā'iyyah): The starting point of a range, e.g., 'one' in 'from one to three'.
الغاية الانتهائية (al-ghāyah al-intihā'iyyah): The endpoint of a range, e.g., 'three' in 'from one to three'.
حظر (hazr): Restriction; the legal principle that in matters of divorce, the minimum number is applied whenever possible.

A) One divorce.
B) Two divorces.
C) Three divorces.
D) No divorce.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi talaq, numeric ranges include the first number but exclude the last, and fractional expressions complete to whole numbers, resulting in a minimum number of divorces.

Fractional Divorce in Hanafi Law: Three Halves and Completion
Explaining the Hanafi ruling on fractional divorce expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of completion (takmil) to fractional divorce expressions and distinguish between the two disputed outcomes for 'three halves of one divorce'.

Core Idea
A half divorce must be completed to a full divorce; three halves of one divorce yield two, but of two divorces yield three.

This passage addresses a subtle issue in Hanafi divorce law: how to count divorces when the husband uses fractional language like 'three halves of two divorces' or 'three halves of one divorce'. The Hanafi school holds that fractions of divorces must be completed (takmil) to form whole divorces, but scholars differ on whether each half completes individually or only after accumulation. This lesson unpacks the two main views, the rebuttals, and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two fractional expressions
The matn presents two phrases: (a) three halves of two divorces (ثلاثة أنصاف طلقتين) and (b) three halves of one divorce (ثلاثة أنصاف طلقة). Note the initial ruling: (a) yields three divorces, (b) yields two divorces according to one view, and three according to another.
2
State the primary dispute for (b)
For 'three halves of one divorce', two views exist: the first view (asahh) treats three halves as 1.5, then completes the half to 2 divorces. The second view treats each half as requiring independent completion, resulting in 3 divorces.
The reasoning: 'كل نصف يتكامل فحصل ثلاث' (each half completes, so three result).
3
Consider the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Their view concerns the expression 'from one to three' (من واحدة إلى ثلاث): they hold that both ends enter, so the first yields two divorces (both one and three apply), and the second yields three. The text explains that the first end must exist for the second to be attached, but because divorce is restricted (حظر), the least is taken.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference; the matn gives one view, but the commentary mentions this alternative.
4
Analyze the asahh (most correct) position
The commentator declares: for (a) three halves of two divorces, the result is three divorces; for (b) three halves of one divorce, two divorces. He rebuts the alternative for (a) by distinguishing between 'half of two divorces' (interpreted as one divorce per half, so three halves = three) and 'half of each divorce' (which would give four halves, but that is not the apparent meaning).
The rebuttal: 'والموجب لأربعة أنصاف إنما هو المعنى الثاني، وهو خلاف الظاهر' (the cause for four halves is only the second meaning, contrary to the apparent).
5
Apply the rule
Given a husband's statement, determine the number of divorces using the asahh: for 'three halves of two divorces' → three divorces; for 'three halves of one divorce' → two divorces. Also note that if the husband intends the unusual interpretation, he is believed in religious matters (صدق ديانة), but the legal ruling is based on apparent meaning.
Practical consequence: the number of divorces affects the possibility of reconciliation and remarriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق / إيقاع الطلاق وفي : من واحدة إلى ثلاث أو ما بين واحدة إلى ثلاث اثنتان ، وبثلاثة أنصاف طلقتين ثلاث وبثلاثةِ أنصاف طلقةٍ طلقتان ، وقيل : ثلاث. وجه الأول : أن ثلاث أنصاف طلقة يكونُ طلقة ونصفاً، فيتكامل النصف، فحصل طلقتان. وجه الثاني : أنَّ كلَّ نصف يتكامل فحصل ثلاث. وعند أبي يوسف ومحمد الله تدخل الغايتان، فتقع في الأولى ثنتان، وفي الثانية ثلاث. وتوجيه قوله : أن الغاية الأولى لا بد أن تكون موجودة لترتب عليه الثانية ، ووجودها بوقوعها، ولا يدخلُ الانتهاء؛ لأنَّ الأصل في الطلاق الحظر، فيحكم بالأقل مهما أمكن. كذا في «البناية)، وغيرها. قوله : وبثلاثة؛ يعني إذا قال : أنت طالق ثلاثة أنصاف طلقتين، يقع ثلاث، وإن قال : ثلاثة أنصاف طلقة واحدة تقعُ اثنتان، وهذا هو الأصح في الصورتين، وقيل: في الصورة الأولى تقعُ ثنتان؛ لأنَّ التطليقتين إذا نصفتا كانت أربعة أنصاف، فثلاثة منها يكون طلقة ونصفاً، فيكمل النصف لعدم التجزؤ، ورد بأنّه فرّق بين نصف التطليقتين ونصف كلّ من التطليقتين، والموجب لأربعة أنصاف إنما هو المعنى الثاني، وهو خلاف الظاهر، نعم اللفظ يحتمله، فلو نواه صدق ديانة، والظاهر هو أن نصف التطليقتين تطليقة، فتقعُ ثلاث بثلاثة أنصاف تطليقتين. وقيل : في الصورة الثانية تقع ثلاث؛ لأنَّ النصف لا بد من تكميله، فثلاثة أنصاف إذا كملت تكون ثلاث تطليقات كوامل
The Book of Divorce / Pronouncement of Divorce. And in [the matn]: 'From one to three or between one and three: two; and with three halves of two divorces: three; and with three halves of one divorce: two divorces, and it is said: three.' The first view: that three halves of one divorce becomes one and a half, so the half is completed, resulting in two divorces. The second view: that each half is completed, resulting in three. And according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them), the two ends enter, so in the first [expression] two occur, and in the second three. And the explanation of his saying: that the first end must exist so that the second can be attached to it, and its existence is by its occurrence, and the end does not enter; because the original principle in divorce is restriction, so the least is ruled whenever possible. This is in al-Binaya and others. His saying: 'and with three'; meaning if he said: 'You are divorced three halves of two divorces,' three occur; and if he said: 'three halves of one divorce,' two occur, and this is the most correct in both cases. And it was said: in the first case two occur, because the two divorces when halved become four halves, so three of them become one and a half, so the half is completed due to indivisibility. And it was rebutted that [this] distinguishes between half of the two divorces and half of each of the two divorces, and the cause for four halves is only the second meaning, which is contrary to the apparent. Yes, the wording admits it, so if he intends it he is believed in religious matters, but the apparent is that half of the two divorces is one divorce, so three occur with three halves of two divorces. And it was said: in the second case three occur, because the half must be completed, so three halves when completed become three complete divorces.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the number of divorces that fall when fractional expressions like 'three halves of two divorces' or 'three halves of one divorce' are used. The matn (base text) states two views: for 'three halves of two divorces' the ruling is three divorces, and for 'three halves of one divorce' it is either two or three. The commentator first gives the reasoning for the two views: one says that three halves of one divorce become one and a half, then the half is completed to make a second divorce, so two; the other says each half is a separate unit, so three halves become three. He then mentions the opinion of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad concerning the expression 'from one to three' where they hold that both ends enter, so the first phrase yields two divorces and the second yields three. The commentator explains that the first end must exist for the second to be attached, so the end does not enter because of the principle of restriction in divorce. Then he returns to the fractional issue, stating that the most correct (asahh) position is: 'three halves of two divorces' yields three, and 'three halves of one divorce' yields two. He rebuts the alternative view that 'three halves of two divorces' yields two, explaining that it confuses halves of the divorces with halves of each divorce. He concludes that if the man intends the unusual interpretation, he is believed in the religious sense, but the apparent meaning is that half of two divorces is one divorce, so three halves give three.

Key Terms
النصف (al-nisf): A fraction of a divorce; half a divorce in the context of fractional pronouncements.
التكميل (al-takmil): The completion of a half to make a full divorce, used in determining the number of divorces from fractional expressions.

A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
When a husband uses fractional divorce expressions, the Hanafi school applies the principle of completion (takmil): halves are combined and then completed to whole divorces, with the asahh ruling depending on whether the half is of one divorce or of two divorces.

Ambiguous Divorce: One in Two
Determining the number of divorces from an ambiguous expression with 'في'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that 'واحدة في ثنتين' yields one divorce and evaluate the opposing view based on arithmetical custom.

Core Idea
Multiplication multiplies parts, not the number; thus, 'one in two' remains one divorce.

When a husband says 'You are divorced one in two', the number of divorces is ambiguous. Does the phrase mean one divorce or two? The Hanafi school argues that multiplication only increases the parts of a single entity, so only one divorce occurs. However, Zufar and the three other Imams rely on arithmetical custom to conclude that two divorces occur. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed phrase
The phrase 'واحدة في ثنتين' (one in two) is ambiguous: it could mean one divorce multiplied by two, or one divorce and two divorces combined.
The preposition 'في' (in) is the key; it may indicate multiplication or combination.
2
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn)
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars, only one divorce occurs, whether the husband intended multiplication or not.
This is the position in the matn (al-Wiqaya).
3
Analyze the proof: multiplication multiplies parts
The Hanafi argument is that multiplication (al-darb) only multiplies the parts of the thing multiplied, not the thing itself. Example: 5 x 4 = 20, but the original unit (5) remains a single quantity.
Thus, multiplying one divorce by two increases its parts, not the number of divorces.
4
Contrast with Zufar and the three Imams
Zufar (a Hanafi) and the three Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that two divorces occur, based on the customary usage of arithmeticians who interpret 'في' as a multiplication sign.
They argue that custom (urf) gives the phrase its meaning.
5
Understand the tarjih (preference) among later Hanafis
Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir preferred Zufar's view because custom is a strong factor, especially when supported by intention. The commentator says this should be the mufta bihi.
This shows that later Hanafi authority can override the earlier matn due to stronger evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي : أنتِ طالق واحدة في ثنتين واحدة نوى الضرب أو لا، قالوا : لأنَّ عمل الضرب في تكثير الأجزاء، لا في زيادة المضروب، وإن نَوَى واحدة وثنتين فثلاث في الموطوءة، وفي غير الموطوءة واحدة وجوابه : أنه لا حاجة إلى تكميل كل نصف على حدة، بل يكمل أحدها بالآخر، فيكون واحداً، ويبقى النصفُ الثالث، فيكمل فيقع اثنان. قوله : وفي أنت طالق ... الخ ; يعني إذا قال : أنت طالق واحدة في ثنتين تقعُ واحدة، سواء نوى بقوله : في ثنتين الضرب، أي ضرب الواحد فيه، أو لم ينو. وعند زفر والأئمة الثلاثة لا تقع ثنتان; لعرف أهل الحساب، فإنهم يريدون بمثل هذا ضرب الواحد في اثنين، وحصول الاثنين منه، وهذا اللفظ حقيقة عرفية في هذا المعنى، فلا بد أن يحمل عليه، لا سيما إذا تأيد ذلك بنية الضرب. ووجه قول أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه : أن عمل الضرب وأثره حقيقة تكثير أجزاء المضروب لا في زيادته، مثلاً إذا ضربنا خمسة في أربعة وحصل عشرون، كان كأَنَّ كلاً من خمسة المضروب جزئ أربعة أجزاء، فمجموع أجزائها صارت عشرين، ومن المعلوم أن الطلقة التي جعلت أجزاؤها كثيرة لا تزيد على طلقة فلا يقع إلا واحد. قوله : قالوا ; أي في توجيه المسألة، وفي مثل هذا التعبير إشارة إلى ضعفه، كيف لا، وهو مخالف للعرف، ومن المعلوم أنّ الاستعمال العرفي قاض في هذا الباب، لا سيما إذا وافقته نية، ولذا رجّحَ ابن الهمام في «الفتح» (١) ، وهو من أهل الترجيح قول زفر الله، فليكن هو المعتمد، وإن كانت المتونُ على خلافه لقوة دليله، والعبرة لقوة الدليل. قوله : فثلاث ; لأنه نوى محتمل كلامه، فإنّ «في» وإن كانت للظرفية لكن قد تستعار بمعنى «الواو»، وهو الجمع المطلق، بناء على أنّ الظرف يجمع المظروف، فيقع ما نوى، هذا إذا كانت المرأة موطوءة صالحة لإيقاع الثلاث عليها متفرقاً، وأمـا غـير الموطوءة فتبين بواحدة ولا عدة لها، فلا تبقى محلاً لوقوع الأخريين.

Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on the ambiguous phrase 'You are divorced one in two' (واحدة في ثنتين). The matn states that one divorce occurs, whether the husband intended multiplication or not. The commentator explains that the action of multiplication only multiplies the parts of the thing, not the thing itself, so the object remains one. However, if the husband intends 'one and two' (i.e., combination), then three divorces occur for a consummated wife because the phrase 'في' can be used in the sense of 'and' (واو) which implies total combination; for an unconsummated wife, only one divorce falls because she is separated by one and has no waiting period. The commentator then notes that Zufar and the three Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that two divorces occur based on the custom of arithmeticians who understand 'واحدة في ثنتين' as multiplication, yielding two. The commentator gives the Hanafi reasoning: multiplication only increases parts, not the whole, so one divorce remains. The commentator then remarks that the expression 'they said' (قالوا) in directing the issue indicates the weakness of that response, because it goes against custom. Therefore, Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir preferred Zufar's view (two divorces) due to the strength of the customary usage, and the commentator says that should be the relied-upon position, even though the texts (mutun) are contrary to it, because the weight is with the strength of the evidence.

Key Terms
الضرب (al-darb): Multiplication (arithmetical operation); used to interpret the phrase 'واحدة في ثنتين' as multiplicand and multiplier.
العرف (al-urf): Custom, specifically the custom of mathematicians who interpret 'in' as multiplication sign.
الموطوءة (al-mawtu'a): A wife with whom consummation has taken place; she is eligible to receive multiple divorces in sequence during a waiting period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the later Hanafi school, how many divorces occur when a husband says 'You are divorced one in two' to a consummated wife without mentioning intention?
A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
When a husband says 'one in two' without intention, the later Hanafi fatwa is two divorces for a consummated wife, but for an unconsummated wife only one divorce occurs because she is separated immediately.

One or Two? The Ruling on 'واحدة في ثنتين' in Divorce
Ruling with proof and disagreement with Zufar and Three Imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the divorce formula 'واحدة في ثنتين' and explain the dispute with Zufar and the Three Imams, including the variant where 'في' means 'and'.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'واحدة في ثنتين' counts as one divorce because multiplication multiplies parts, not the essence.

The divorce formula 'واحدة في ثنتين' appears to be a mathematical expression, but its legal effect depends on how 'في' is interpreted. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi view that only one talaq occurs, and contrasts it with the opposing view based on customary mathematical usage. You'll also learn how the ruling changes when the husband intends 'one and two' instead of multiplication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the formula and core ruling
The husband says 'أنت طالق واحدة في ثنتين'. First, determine the literal meaning: 'You are divorced once in two'. The Hanafi position is that only one divorce occurs, regardless of intention to multiply.
The ruling applies whether the husband intended multiplication or not.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof using multiplication of parts
The Hanafi argument: multiplication multiplies the parts of a number, not the number itself. For example, 5×4=20 means each unit of 5 is divided into 4 parts, but the essence remains 5. Similarly, one divorce with multiplied parts remains one divorce.
This is based on a linguistic understanding of the operation of multiplication.
3
Compare with Zufar and the Three Imams' proof
Zufar and the Three Imams (al-Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) argue that customary mathematical usage treats 'one in two' as multiplication (1×2=2), so two divorces occur, especially if the husband intended multiplication.
They prioritize customary reality (حقيقة عرفية) over the Hanafi linguistic interpretation.
4
Distinguish the variant where 'في' means 'and'
If the husband intends 'واحدة وثنتين' (one and two) by using 'في' in the sense of 'مع', then for a consummated woman three divorces occur because he intended three. For a non-consummated woman, only one divorce occurs because she becomes irrevocably divorced immediately.
This shows the Hanafi commitment to interpreting ambiguous expressions according to probable intention.

And: 'You are divorced one in two' is one, whether he intended multiplication or not. They said: because the operation of multiplication multiplies the parts, not the thing multiplied. And if he intended one and two, then three in the consummated woman, and in the non-consummated woman one. Its answer: There is no need to complete each half separately, but one completes the other, so it becomes one, and the third half remains, so it completes and two occur. [1] His saying: 'And: You are divorced...' etc.; meaning if he says: 'You are divorced one in two', one divorce occurs, whether he intended by 'in two' multiplication, i.e., multiplying the one in it, or not. According to Zufar and the three Imams, two do not occur, due to the custom of mathematicians, for they intend by such an expression multiplication of one by two and obtaining two from it, and this verbal expression is a customary reality in this meaning, so it must be interpreted accordingly, especially if supported by the intention of multiplication. And the view of Abu Hanifa and his two companions: that the operation of multiplication and its effect is really the multiplication of the parts of the multiplied, not an increase in it; for example, if we multiply five by four and obtain twenty, it is as if each of the five multiplied is divided into four parts, so the total of its parts becomes twenty, and it is known that a divorce that has many parts does not increase beyond one divorce, so only one occurs. [2] His saying: 'they said' i.e., in explaining the issue, and in such an expression there is an indication of its weakness; why not, and it contradicts custom, and it is known that customary usage is decisive in this matter, especially if it agrees with intention; therefore, Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath (1), who is among the scholars of preference, preferred the view of Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him), so let it be the relied-upon view, even if the texts are contrary to it due to the strength of his proof, and consideration is for the strength of proof. (1) [3] His saying: 'then three' because he intended a possible meaning of his speech. For 'in' even if it is for containment, it may be used metaphorically in the sense of 'and', and that is absolute conjunction, based on the fact that the container gathers the contained, so what he intended occurs. This is if the woman is consummated, eligible for the occurrence of three divorces upon her separately. As for the non-consummated woman, she becomes irrevocably divorced by one and has no 'idda, so she no longer remains a locus for the occurrence of the other two. 10 (1) Fath al-Qadir (4): 23, and the speech of Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar (2: 439) leans towards it.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's ruling for 'واحدة في ثنتين'. The author first presents the ruling: if a husband says 'أنت طالق واحدة في ثنتين', only one talaq occurs, regardless of whether he intended multiplication. This is the Hanafi position of Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The passage then introduces the opposition: Zufar and the Three Imams (al-Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) argue that two talaqs occur, because in mathematical custom 'one in two' means multiplication (1×2=2), and this customary usage should prevail, especially if the husband intended multiplication. The Hanafi response is based on a key distinction: multiplication multiplies the parts of a number, not the number itself. For example, 5×4 yields 20, but that 20 is composed of 5 taken 4 times; similarly, one talaq multiplied in expression remains one talaq, just with many parts. The commentary then notes that Ibn al-Humam in al-Fath actually preferred Zufar's view due to the strength of customary evidence, but the matn texts follow the Hanafi view. The passage then addresses the variant where the husband intends 'واحدة وثنتين' (one and two) by using 'في' in the sense of 'مع' (with/and). In that case, for a consummated woman, three talaqs occur because the husband intended three. For a non-consummated woman, only one talaq occurs because she becomes irrevocably divorced immediately and cannot receive further divorces. This shows the Hanafi commitment to interpreting ambiguous expressions according to probable intention, while also respecting the legal consequences of consummation.

Key Terms
في (fi): In this context, used for multiplication (ضرب) or as a conjunction meaning 'with/and' (مع/واو).
الضرب (al-darb): Multiplication operation; the author argues it multiplies parts, not the whole.
الموطوءة (al-mawtu'a): A woman who has been consummated (i.e., marital intercourse has occurred).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Two divorces, because intention clarifies the ambiguous expression.
C) Three divorces, because of the consummation condition.
D) The divorce is invalid due to ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
The divorce formula 'واحدة في ثنتين' counts as one talaq in the Hanafi school because multiplication only multiplies parts, not the divorce itself.

Ruling on 'Two in Two' Divorce Multiplication
Commentary on the ruling of 'two in two' divorce formulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of multiplication in divorce formulations to determine the number of divorces, and distinguish it from Zufar's customary interpretation.

Core Idea
Multiplication in 'two in two' yields two divorces, not four or three.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the ambiguous phrase 'ثنتين في ثنتين'. The commentator explains that when the husband intends multiplication, only two divorces occur. Zufar's opposing view, based on customary understanding, is also presented and rebutted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the formulation
Recognize the phrase 'ثنتين في ثنتين' as a potential multiplication of divorces.
The key word is 'في' which here indicates multiplication.
2
Determine the husband's intention
The ruling depends on his intent: if he intends الضرب (multiplication), the Hanafi rule applies.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
Multiplication multiplies parts of the number, not individual units; thus 'two in two' means two parts of two, i.e., two divorces.
This is based on linguistic analysis, not custom.
4
Consider Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that customary interpretation would yield four divorces, but since three is the maximum, three occur.
He relies on the principle that custom (عرف) can override literal meaning.
5
Understand the Hanafi response
Custom does not override the clear linguistic principle; the added fourth is ignored because it exceeds the limit, leaving only two.
The Hanafi rebuttal asserts the primacy of linguistic rules over custom in this context.

Like: one and two, and if he intended 'with two' then three, and 'two in two' and intended multiplication, two. And 'from here to Syria' one revocable divorce. [1] His saying: 'like...etc.' means the difference in the ruling of the consummated and the non-consummated here is like the difference in the case where he uses the word 'and', saying: 'You are divorced one and two', then for a consummated woman three occur, for a non-consummated one one, contrary to if he pronounces three divorces at once i.e., with one word, then they all occur upon her. [2] His saying: 'as if he said' indicates that the saying 'like one and two' is attached to the last case, and it can be attached to both, as we have indicated. [3] His saying: 'then three' whether she is consummated or not, because the non-consummated woman becomes irrevocably divorced by one only if he pronounces three divorces separately. But if he pronounces them together like saying 'You are divorced three' or 'one with two', she becomes irrevocably divorced by all. [4] His saying: 'and two in two...etc.' means: if he says 'You are divorced two in two' intending multiplication, two occur, because as passed, multiplication only multiplies the parts of the multiplied, not its individuals. According to Zufar, three occur, because the import of this expression, according to custom, requires the occurrence of four, and it is known that there is no increase in divorce beyond three, so the fourth is disregarded and three remain. [0] His saying: 'one revocable divorce'; and Zufar said: irrevocable, because he described the divorce with length (by saying 'from here to the kingdom of Syria'), so it is taken as intensification. And we say: divorce, whenever it occurs, (1) because the word 'in' comes to mean 'with'; Allah Most High said: 'Enter among My servants, and enter My Garden' [Quran 89:29-30]. See: al-Tabyin (2:203). (2) Because he described it with shortness; because whenever it occurs in a place, it occurs in all places; so specifying Syria is a shortening relative to beyond it, then it does not actually admit of shortness, so it is a shortening of its ruling, and it is revocable, not irrevocable; and because he did not describe her with greatness or magnitude but extended it to a place, and that does not admit it, so no increase of intensity is established. See: Radd al-Muhtar (3:261-262). And its length.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on multiple divorce formulations. The matn lists several cases: (1) 'واحدة وثنتين' (one and two) – if the husband intends 'مع ثنتين' (with two), then three divorces occur for a consummated woman, one for a non-consummated woman. The commentator explains that this is analogous to using 'and' (واو) between numbers. (2) 'ثنتين في ثنتين' (two in two) – if intended as multiplication, then two divorces occur, because multiplication only multiples parts, not individuals. Zufar, however, says three occur, because the custom implies four, but since three is the maximum, four reduces to three. (3) 'من هنا إلى الشام' (from here to Syria) – one revocable (رجعية) divorce. Zufar considers it irrevocable (بائنة) because the spatial description suggests intensification. The Hanafi rebuttal: divorce occurs in all places; specifying a place is a restriction not on the act but on its ruling, and since the description is of length, it does not increase severity; hence it remains revocable. The commentator also provides linguistic evidence that 'في' can mean 'مع' (with), citing Quran 89:29-30. This passage shows how the Hanafi school interprets ambiguous divorce formulas by relying on linguistic principles, intention, and the legal effect of consummation.

Key Terms
رجعية (raj'iyyah): A revocable divorce, where the husband may take back his wife during the 'idda period without a new marriage contract.
الموطوءة (al-mawtu'a): A consummated woman (intercourse has occurred).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Two divorces
B) Three divorces
C) Four divorces
D) One divorce

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, 'two in two' with intent of multiplication equals two divorces; the custom of reading it as four is overridden by the linguistic principle of multiplication.

Ruling on 'From Here to Syria' Spatial Divorce
Commentary on the ruling of spatial divorce formula 'from here to Syria'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the spatial divorce formula 'from here to Syria' and explain why it remains revocable despite Zufar's argument for irrevocability.

Core Idea
Describing divorce with a location does not intensify it; it remains revocable.

This lesson addresses the phrase 'من هنا إلى الشام'. The Hanafi school rules it as one revocable divorce, while Zufar considers it irrevocable due to perceived intensification through spatial description. The commentary provides linguistic and rational arguments for the Hanafi view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the formula
Recognize the phrase 'من هنا إلى الشام' as a spatial description of divorce.
The phrase literally means 'from here to Syria'.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The matn rules it as one revocable (raji'iyyah) divorce.
3
Understand Zufar's argument
Zufar argues that the spatial description implies length and intensification, making the divorce irrevocable (ba'in).
He uses the principle that describing divorce with something intense increases its severity.
4
Analyze Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is multi-pronged: (a) 'في' can mean 'مع' (with), as in Quran 89:29-30; (b) specifying a place is a restriction on the ruling, not an intensification; (c) length does not imply severity because divorce occurs in all places—specifying a place is a shortening, not lengthening.
The commentary also notes that if he had said 'to the sky', it might have indicated intensity, but 'to Syria' is ordinary.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: revocable allows taking back the wife during 'idda; irrevocable requires a new marriage contract.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۲) لأنه وصفه بالقصر ؛ لأنه متى وقع في مكان وقع في كل الأماكن ؛ فتخصيصه بالشام تقصير بالنسبة إلى ما وراءه، ثم لا يحتمل القصر حقيقة، فكان قصر حكمه، وهو بالرجعي ، بالبائن ؛ ولأنه لم يصفها بعظم ولا كبر بل مدها إلى مكان، وهو لا يحتمله ، فلم يثبت به زيادة شدة. ينظر : رد المحتار (٣: ٢٦١ - ٢٦٢). وطوله
Like: one and two, and if he intended 'with two' then three, and 'two in two' and intended multiplication, two. And 'from here to Syria' one revocable divorce. [1] His saying: 'like...etc.' means the difference in the ruling of the consummated and the non-consummated here is like the difference in the case where he uses the word 'and', saying: 'You are divorced one and two', then for a consummated woman three occur, for a non-consummated one one, contrary to if he pronounces three divorces at once i.e., with one word, then they all occur upon her. [2] His saying: 'as if he said' indicates that the saying 'like one and two' is attached to the last case, and it can be attached to both, as we have indicated. [3] His saying: 'then three' whether she is consummated or not, because the non-consummated woman becomes irrevocably divorced by one only if he pronounces three divorces separately. But if he pronounces them together like saying 'You are divorced three' or 'one with two', she becomes irrevocably divorced by all. [4] His saying: 'and two in two...etc.' means: if he says 'You are divorced two in two' intending multiplication, two occur, because as passed, multiplication only multiplies the parts of the multiplied, not its individuals. According to Zufar, three occur, because the import of this expression, according to custom, requires the occurrence of four, and it is known that there is no increase in divorce beyond three, so the fourth is disregarded and three remain. [0] His saying: 'one revocable divorce'; and Zufar said: irrevocable, because he described the divorce with length (by saying 'from here to the kingdom of Syria'), so it is taken as intensification. And we say: divorce, whenever it occurs, (1) because the word 'in' comes to mean 'with'; Allah Most High said: 'Enter among My servants, and enter My Garden' [Quran 89:29-30]. See: al-Tabyin (2:203). (2) Because he described it with shortness; because whenever it occurs in a place, it occurs in all places; so specifying Syria is a shortening relative to beyond it, then it does not actually admit of shortness, so it is a shortening of its ruling, and it is revocable, not irrevocable; and because he did not describe her with greatness or magnitude but extended it to a place, and that does not admit it, so no increase of intensity is established. See: Radd al-Muhtar (3:261-262). And its length.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on multiple divorce formulations. The matn lists several cases: (1) 'واحدة وثنتين' (one and two) – if the husband intends 'مع ثنتين' (with two), then three divorces occur for a consummated woman, one for a non-consummated woman. The commentator explains that this is analogous to using 'and' (واو) between numbers. (2) 'ثنتين في ثنتين' (two in two) – if intended as multiplication, then two divorces occur, because multiplication only multiples parts, not individuals. Zufar, however, says three occur, because the custom implies four, but since three is the maximum, four reduces to three. (3) 'من هنا إلى الشام' (from here to Syria) – one revocable (رجعية) divorce. Zufar considers it irrevocable (بائنة) because the spatial description suggests intensification. The Hanafi rebuttal: divorce occurs in all places; specifying a place is a restriction not on the act but on its ruling, and since the description is of length, it does not increase severity; hence it remains revocable. The commentator also provides linguistic evidence that 'في' can mean 'مع' (with), citing Quran 89:29-30. This passage shows how the Hanafi school interprets ambiguous divorce formulas by relying on linguistic principles, intention, and the legal effect of consummation.

Key Terms
رجعية (raj'iyyah): A revocable divorce, where the husband may take back his wife during the 'idda period without a new marriage contract.
بائنة (ba'inah): An irrevocable divorce, where the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage contract and her consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Spatial descriptions in divorce formulas do not intensify the divorce; the Hanafi school maintains it as one revocable talaq.

Hanafi Rulings on Ambiguous Divorce Pronouncements: 'واحدة وثنتين' and 'ثنتين في ثنتين'
Expansion of a matn rule on ambiguous talaq phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position for each ambiguous divorce expression and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with Zufar on the number of divorces and revocability.

Core Idea
In 'two in two', Hanafi says two divorces occur—multiplication increases parts, not individuals.

This lesson unpacks the legal implications of complex talaq phrases where the husband uses words like 'in' (في) or 'from...to'. The Hanafi masters, especially Abu Hanifa and his students, debated whether such expressions multiply the count or merely emphasize. The passage clarifies that linguistic precision dictates the outcome: 'في' can mean 'with' and الضرب (multiplication) only duplicates components, not independent divorces. Understanding this requires grappling with both the matn and the sharh commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expressions
List the three key utterances: 'واحدة في ثنتين', 'ثنتين في ثنتين', and 'من هنا إلى السام'.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
For non-consummated wife: one divorce (unless intending three). For 'two in two': two divorces. For 'from here to Syria': one revocable divorce.
3
Note Zufar's dissenting view
Zufar holds three divorces in 'two in two' (because custom implies four, then one is nullified) and irrevocable in the location expression (because length implies severity).
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
Multiplication only increases parts, not legal entities; 'في' in 'واحدة في ثنتين' means 'with' (مع). For 'من هنا إلى السام', divorce is not spatially constrained, so specification shortens scope—hence revocable.
See the dalil from Quran 89:29-30 for 'في' as 'مع'.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the wife becomes irrevocably divorced (three) or remains within revocable limit (two). In the location case, it affects the right to return without new marriage.

Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage from the sharh explains the ruling on ambiguous divorce expressions. The matn states that if a man says to a non-consummated wife 'You are divorced one in two' and intends 'one and two', only one divorce occurs. This is analogous to saying 'one and two' without 'in' for a non-consummated wife. If he intends 'with two' (i.e., combining them), then three occur. For the expression 'two in two' intending multiplication, two occur because multiplication only increases parts, not individual divorces. The Hanafi position is that only two divorces occur; Zufar holds that three occur because the expression implies four but divorce cannot exceed three, so three remain. On the expression 'from here to Syria for a revocable divorce', Zufar says it is irrevocable because describing the divorce as long implies severity. The Hanafi response is that divorce is not restricted by location, so the specification of Syria shortens the scope, implying revocability, not severity. The proofs rely on linguistic analysis: 'in' can mean 'with' as in Quran 89:29-30, and multiplication does not create new entities.

Key Terms
في (fi): Used as 'with' (مع) in the expression 'واحدة في ثنتين'
الضرب (al-darb): Multiplication, as in 'ثنتين في ثنتين' meaning 2 x 2
رجعية (raj'iyyah): Revocable divorce, where husband can take wife back without new contract
بائنة (ba'inah): Irrevocable divorce requiring new marriage contract

Key Takeaway
In complex divorce expressions, the Hanafi school uses linguistic principles—where 'في' can mean 'with' and multiplication only increases parts, not independent divorces—to determine the count, rejecting overreliance on custom.

Immediate vs Conditional Divorce: Spatial Expressions
Clarification that spatial expressions do not delay divorce unless conditional wording is used.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between immediate (tunjeez) and conditional (ta'leeq) divorce when spatial expressions are used, and apply the Hanafi rule that specifying a place does not delay divorce.

Core Idea
A spatial phrase like 'at Mecca' does not make divorce conditional; it remains immediate unless a conditional particle is used.

This passage clarifies a common scenario in divorce: when a husband uses a place or time expression like 'at Mecca' or 'in the shade'. The Hanafi ruling is that such expressions do not delay the divorce—it takes effect immediately. However, if the husband genuinely intended to condition the divorce on entry, his inner intention is accepted religiously but not in court. This lesson walks through the key distinctions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression used
Look at the wording: does it use a spatial phrase like 'بمكة' (at Mecca) or a conditional phrase like 'إذا دخلت مكة' (if you enter Mecca)? The first step is to classify the expression as either a locative (ظرف) or a conditional clause.
2
Determine if the locative is genuine or metaphorical
If the phrase is a genuine locative (e.g., 'في الظل'), it cannot serve as a condition, so the divorce is immediate. If the phrase is not a genuine locative because it cannot contain an event (e.g., 'في دخولك'), then 'في' is used metaphorically to mean 'if', making the divorce conditional.
Genuine locatives: places or times that can contain an event. Metaphorical: actions or states that cannot contain an event.
3
Apply the default rule: spatial = immediate
Hanafi principle: divorce is not specific to any place or time. Therefore, saying 'أنت طالق بمكة' (you are divorced at Mecca) does not postpone the divorce; it is effective immediately wherever the wife is. The specification is nullified (يلغو تخصيصه).
Exception: if the husband intended conditional by deleting the word 'entry' (e.g., meaning 'upon your entry into Mecca'), he is believed in religious matters but not in court.
4
Recognise clear conditional phrases
Explicit conditional particles like 'إذا' (if, when) or 'في' used metaphorically for 'if' (when it is not a genuine locative) make the divorce conditional. For example, 'إذا دخلت مكة' or 'في دخولك الدار' clearly tie the divorce to a future event.
In these cases, the divorce only occurs upon the happening of the condition, and this is enforced in court.
5
Note the distinction between din and qada
If the husband uses a spatial expression but inwardly intended conditional, his claim is accepted religiously (he is not sinful if he does not treat it as immediate) but not in court (the judge rules it immediate due to the apparent wording).
This is based on the principle that court rulings follow apparent evidence, while religious accountability considers hidden intentions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينجز الطلاق في بمكة، أو في مكة، أو في الدار، وعُلّق في : إذا دخلت مكة ، أو في دخولك الدار.
[1]
وينجز الطلاق في بمكة، أو في مكة، أو في الدار) : أي إذا قال : أنتِ طالق بمكة، أو في مكة، فهو تنجيز).
(وعُلّق في : إذا دخلت مكة ، أو في دخولك الدار).
في مكان وقع في جميع الأماكن ؛ لعدم تخصصه بمكان دون مكان ، فلما قال : إلى الشام وصفه بالقصر ؛ لأنه لا يحتمل ما وراءه ، فيحمل على الطلاق القاصر وهو الرجعي. [ 1 ] قوله : ويُنجز ؛ مضارع مجهول من التنجيز ، وهو عبارة عن إيقاعه في الحال ، ويقابله التعليق، كقوله : أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار.
[٢] قوله : طالق بمكة ؛ وكذا : أنت طالق في الظلّ أو في الشمس، أو أنت طالق مريضة، أو مصلية، أو نحو ذلك ؛ وذلك لأنّ الطلاق لا يختص وقوعه بمكان دون مكان، وحال دون حال، فمتى وقع وقع في الأمكنة والأزمنة والأحوال كلّها، فيلغو تخصيصه، نعم أراد به التعليق، بأن يحذف المضاف أي في دخولك بمكة ونحوه، يصدق ديانة لا قضاء. كذا في «التلويح». [٣] قوله : وعُلّق ؛ بصيغة المجهول من التعليق، يعني إذا قال : أنت طالق إذا دخلت مكة، أو في دخولك مكة، أو الدار، يكون تعليقاً بناء على أنّ «في» قد تستعارُ للمقارنة إذا لم تصلح ظرفاً، فيصير بمعنى الشرط ، فيكون تعليقاً، كذا قال الشارح له في تنقيح الأصول» (٢).
And divorce is made immediate in 'at Mecca', or 'in Mecca', or 'in the house', and it is made conditional in 'if you enter Mecca', or 'in your entering the house'. [1] (And divorce is made immediate in 'at Mecca', or 'in Mecca', or 'in the house'): meaning if he says: You are divorced at Mecca, or in Mecca, it is immediate. (And it is made conditional in: if you enter Mecca, or in your entering the house). Because when it occurs, it occurs in all places; since it is not specific to one place over another, so when he said 'to Syria', he described it as short because it cannot bear what is beyond it, so it is taken as a short divorce, which is revocable. [1] His saying: 'is made immediate' is a passive present from al-tanjeez, which means effecting it instantly, and its opposite is al-ta'leeq, like his saying: You are divorced if you enter the house. [2] His saying: 'divorced at Mecca' and likewise: you are divorced in the shade or in the sun, or you are divorced sick, or praying, or similar; that is because divorce is not specific in its occurrence to a place over another place, nor a state over another state, so whenever it occurs, it occurs in all places and times and states, so its specification is nullified. However, if he intended conditional, by deleting the annexed word, i.e., 'in your entering Mecca' and similar, he is believed in religious matters but not in court. This is in 'al-Talwih'. [3] His saying: 'and it is made conditional' is in the passive from al-ta'leeq, meaning if he says: You are divorced if you enter Mecca, or in your entering Mecca, or the house, it is made conditional, based on the fact that 'in' is used metaphorically for concomitance when it is not suitable as a locative, so it becomes a conditional, as the commentator said in 'Tanqih al-Usul'.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction between immediate (tunjeez) and conditional (ta'leeq) divorce when spatial expressions are used. The matn states that saying 'You are divorced at Mecca' or 'in Mecca' is an immediate divorce, not postponed until arrival. The reason is that divorce does not attach to any specific location; it occurs instantly wherever the wife is. Specifying a place is thus meaningless. However, if the husband intended to make it conditional, e.g., by deleting the word 'dakhol' (entering) and meaning 'upon your entrance into Mecca', then his religious sincerity is accepted but not in court (qada'), because the apparent wording indicates immediacy. On the other hand, expressions like 'if you enter Mecca' or 'in your entering the house' clearly indicate conditionality. The word 'في' (in) can be used metaphorically to mean 'if' when it cannot serve as a genuine locative. The proof is linguistic: divorce is not restricted by place, time, or condition unless explicitly conditional.

Key Terms
تنجيز (tunjeez): Immediate effect of divorce without any condition
تعليق (ta'leeq): Conditional divorce, where occurrence depends on a future event
في (fi): Used as conditional when not a valid locative, equivalent to 'if'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One immediate divorce
B) One divorce when she is in the shade
C) Three immediate divorces
D) No divorce because it is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, spatial expressions in divorce are treated as immediate (tunjeez) unless a clear conditional particle like 'if' is used.

Spatial Addition in Talaq: Immediate vs. Conditional
Explains that specifying a place for talaq does not delay it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on specifying a place in talaq, explain why it is immediate (tawjīz), and distinguish between the judicial (qaḍā') and religious (diyānatan) rulings.

Core Idea
Talaq is not restricted to any place; naming a place does not delay the divorce.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya explains a key principle in Hanafi talaq: spatial terms like 'in Mecca' do not suspend divorce. The husband's intention to make it conditional may be accepted religiously, but the judge rules by the apparent wording. Understanding this distinction is crucial for both legal practice and personal accountability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two expressions
Recognize that the source contrasts spatial phrases (e.g., 'in Mecca') with conditional phrases (e.g., 'if you enter Mecca').
2
State the ruling for each
Spatial phrases produce immediate divorce (tawjīz); conditional phrases produce suspended divorce (ta'līq).
3
Understand the proof
The proof is rational: talaq is not specific to any place, time, or state, so specifying a place is legally meaningless; the divorce occurs at the moment of utterance.
4
Recognize the diyāna/qaḍā' distinction
If the husband intended the spatial phrase as a condition (e.g., by omitting the implied verbal noun), it is accepted religiously (diyānatan) but not judicially (qaḍā'an) because the apparent wording indicates immediacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينجز الطلاق في بمكة، أو في مكة، أو في الدار، وعُلّق في : إذا دخلت مكة ، أو في دخولك الدار.
[1] وينجز الطلاق في بمكة، أو في مكة، أو في الدار) : أي إذا قال : أنتِ طالق بمكة، أو في مكة، فهو تنجيز).
(وعُلّق في : إذا دخلت مكة ، أو في دخولك الدار). في مكان وقع في جميع الأماكن ؛ لعدم تخصصه بمكان دون مكان ، فلما قال : إلى الشام وصفه بالقصر ؛ لأنه لا يحتمل ما وراءه، فيحمل على الطلاق القاصر وهو الرجعي. [ 1 ] قوله : ويُنجز ؛ مضارع مجهول من التنجيز، وهو عبارة عن إيقاعه في الحال، ويقابله التعليق، كقوله : أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار.
[٢] قوله : طالق بمكة ؛ وكذا : أنت طالق في الظلّ أو في الشمس، أو أنت طالق مريضة، أو مصلية، أو نحو ذلك ؛ وذلك لأنّ الطلاق لا يختص وقوعه بمكان دون مكان، وحال دون حال، فمتى وقع وقع في الأمكنة والأزمنة والأحوال كلّها، فيلغو تخصيصه، نعم أراد به التعليق، بأن يحذف المضاف أي في دخولك بمكة ونحوه، يصدق ديانة لا قضاء. كذا في «التلويح». [٣] قوله : وعُلّق ؛ بصيغة المجهول من التعليق، يعني إذا قال : أنت طالق إذا دخلت مكة، أو في دخولك مكة، أو الدار، يكون تعليقاً بناء على أنّ «في» قد تستعارُ للمقارنة إذا لم تصلح ظرفاً، فيصير بمعنى الشرط ، فيكون تعليقاً، كذا قال الشارح له في تنقيح الأصول.
Talaq is immediate when [the husband says] 'in Mecca', or 'in Mecca', or 'in the house', and it is suspended when [he says] 'if you enter Mecca', or 'upon your entering the house'. [Commentary:] (Talaq is immediate in 'in Mecca' or 'in Mecca' or 'in the house'): meaning if he says 'you are divorced in Mecca' or 'in Mecca', it is immediate. (And suspended in: 'if you enter Mecca' or 'upon your entering the house'). [If he says it] in a place, it occurs in all places because it is not specific to any place. When he said 'to Sham', it is described as restrictive because it does not admit beyond it, so it is interpreted as a restricted divorce, which is revocable. [1] His saying 'it is immediate' is a passive imperfect from tawjīz, which means executing it immediately, and its opposite is suspension, like his saying 'you are divorced if you enter the house.' [2] His saying 'divorced in Mecca'; similarly, 'you are divorced in the shade or in the sun', or 'you are divorced while sick' or 'while praying' or similar; because divorce is not specific to any place or state, so whenever it occurs, it occurs in all places, times, and states, so the specification is negated. However, if he intended suspension by deleting the governed noun, i.e., 'upon your entering Mecca' and the like, it is accepted religiously but not judicially. Thus in al-Talwīh. [3] His saying 'and suspended' in the passive form from suspension means if he says 'you are divorced if you enter Mecca' or 'upon your entering Mecca' or 'the house', it is suspension based on the fact that 'fī' is sometimes borrowed for association when it is not suitable as a place adverb, so it becomes in the meaning of a condition, thus it is suspension. So said the commentator in Tanqīh al-Usūl.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya explains the effect of specifying a place in the pronouncement of talaq. The author begins by stating the ruling: if a husband says 'you are divorced in Mecca' or 'in the house', the talaq is immediate (tawjīz). The reason is that talaq, by its nature, is not restricted to any particular place, time, or state. Therefore, specifying a place is legally meaningless; the divorce takes effect at the moment of utterance. In contrast, if the husband uses a conditional expression like 'if you enter Mecca' or 'upon your entering the house', it becomes a suspended talaq (ta'līq), which takes effect only upon the occurrence of the condition. The commentary further explains that the word 'fī' (in) can be used figuratively to indicate a condition, leading to suspension. Importantly, if the husband intends the spatial phrase as a condition (e.g., by omitting the implied verbal noun), his intention is accepted religiously (diyānatan) but not judicially (qaḍā'an) because the apparent wording indicates immediacy. This distinction between diyāna and qaḍā' is crucial: the judge must rule based on the literal meaning, while the husband's inner intention is between him and Allah.

Key Terms
تنجيز (tawjīz): Immediate execution of divorce at the moment of utterance.
تعليق (ta'līq): Suspending the divorce on the occurrence of a future condition.
ديانة (diyānatan): Religiously, according to the person's inner intention before Allah.
قضاء (qaḍā'an): Judicially, the ruling of the judge based on apparent evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a husband says 'you are divorced in Mecca' (أنتِ طالق في مكة), what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is conditional and takes effect upon entering Mecca.
B) It is an immediate divorce (tawjīz).
C) It is invalid because the place is not specified.
D) It is delayed until the time he actually goes to Mecca.

Key Takeaway
Specifying a place in talaq does not delay its effect; the divorce is immediate unless a conditional expression is used or the husband sincerely intended a condition (accepted only religiously).

Divorce Timing: 'Tomorrow' vs 'In Tomorrow' and Intention
Hanafi dispute on divorce time with 'غداً' vs 'في غد' and intention validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce timing with 'غداً' vs 'في غد' and distinguish the judicial validity of intention between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Without 'في', divorce covers the whole day; with 'في', intention for a part is valid in court.

The timing of a divorce pronouncement depends on the exact phrasing used. A single word or particle can change whether the husband's intention to delay the effect is accepted in a court of law. This passage examines the critical distinction between saying 'tomorrow' (غداً) and 'in tomorrow' (في غد) and how the Hanafi imams differ on the judicial validity of intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrasings and basic ruling
Recognize that both 'أنت طالق غداً' and 'أنت طالق في غد' result in divorce occurring at dawn. The dispute concerns whether the husband's intention to specify a later part (e.g., Asr) is accepted in court.
2
Understand the linguistic difference
Analyze why 'غداً' without 'في' functions like a direct object (مفعول فيه مشبه بالمفعول به) requiring comprehensiveness (استيعاب), while 'في غد' treats tomorrow as a mere container (ظرف) allowing a part.
The key is the particle 'في' — its presence or absence transforms the grammatical role.
3
Apply the analogy to fasting
Examine the example: 'صمت السنة' (I fasted the year) means every day; 'صمت في السنة' (I fasted in the year) allows a single day. This parallels the divorce case and illustrates the linguistic rule.
The analogy strengthens Abu Hanifa's argument for the effect of 'في'.
4
Contrast Abu Hanifa's view with the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa: intention is valid judicially only when 'في' is used. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: intention is not valid in either phrasing. Note that all agree on religious validity (diyānatan).
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is in court acceptance.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Apply the ruling: if a husband says 'في غد' and claims he intended a specific time, Abu Hanifa accepts this in court; the Sahibayn do not. The divorce is effective from dawn in all cases judicially for them.
Understanding this helps predict court rulings in such cases.
