The Legal Meaning of 'Day' in Divorce Oaths
Definition and ruling on the word 'day' in divorce oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for interpreting 'اليوم' in divorce oaths and identify when contextual clues override the literal meaning.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'اليوم' in divorce oaths means the daytime, not the full 24 hours, unless context indicates otherwise.

When a husband says 'You are divorced today,' does the divorce take effect at any time during the day or only during daylight? The Hanafi school has a precise linguistic principle: the word 'day' (اليوم) in such statements is literal for the daytime (النهار). However, this rule has exceptions when contextual clues suggest a different meaning. This passage from a major Hanafi commentary explains the rule, addresses objections, and clarifies when it can be overridden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
What is the rule about the word 'اليوم' in divorce statements? The text states: 'واليوم للنهار' — the day is for the daytime.
2
Understand the literal meaning
Why is 'اليوم' considered literal for daytime and not the 24-hour day? Because it is contrasted with 'الليل' (night) and is defined as the period of light.
3
Examine the first objection
The objection: Quran 8:16 uses 'يوم' to mean absolute time (any time of day or night). Does that force a metaphorical reading that would combine literal and metaphorical? The answer: It is purely metaphorical, no combination is intended.
The Hanafi response avoids the forbidden combination of literal and metaphorical in one usage.
4
Examine the second objection
The objection: Some phrases like 'ride on the day the enemy comes' extend beyond daytime, while others like 'you are divorced on the day Zayd fasts' are limited to daytime. The answer: The literal rule applies only when there are no contextual clues (قرائن). With clues, the meaning can shift.
Al-Taftazani in al-Talwih is cited for this resolution.
5
Apply the rule
Given the rule and its exception, in which cases does the literal meaning hold? When there is no contextual clue forcing a different interpretation. Example: 'You are divorced today' without qualification means the daytime of that day.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنهار
( واليوم
(۱) (۲) للنهار
٢٥٣
(1) قوله : واليوم... الخ؛ شروع في ذكر ضابطة تتفرع منها فروع كثيرة في هذا البحث، ويرد عليه بوجهين :
أحدهما : أنهم صرّحوا بأنّ اليوم حقيقة في النهار ؛ ولذا يقابل بالليل، وهو المراد في قولهم : اليوم بليلة ومجاز في مطلق الوقت، كما في قوله الله : وَمَن يُوَلِّهِمْ يَوْمَين
دُبُرَهُ إِلَّا مُتَحَرِّفا لِقِتَالٍ أَوْ مُتَحَيّرًا إِلَى فِئَةٍ فَقَدْ بَاءَ بِغَضَبٍ مِنَ اللَّهِ .
فإنّ المراد باليوم هاهنا مطلق الوقت لظهور أنّ التولّي عن الزحف والفرار عن القتال حرام في كل وقت، ليلاً كان أو نهاراً ، فإذا أريد باليوم مطلق الوقت يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز. والجواب عنه : أن في صورة حمل اليوم على الوقت حمل الكلام على معناه المجازي فقط، لا جمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، فإنّه لا يراد به النهار والليل كلاهما، بل مطلق الوقت، وهو معنى مجازي، ولا يلزم من شموله للنّهار الجمع الممنوع.
وثانيهما : إنّ هذه الضابطة تنتقض في كثير من الصور، فكثيراً ما يمتد الفعل مع كون اليوم لمطلق الوقت ، مثل : اركبوا يوم يأتيكم العدو وأحسنوا الظن بالله يوم يأتيكم الموت، وكثيراً ما لا يمتد الفعل مع كون اليوم كبياض النهار، مثل أنت طالق يوم يصوم زيد، وأنت حرّة يوم تكسف الشمس. والجواب عنه على ما ذكره التفتازاني له في «التلويح»، وغيره : أن الحكم
(۲)
المذكور إنما هو عند الإطلاق والخلو عن الموانع والقرائن، فلا تمتنع مخالفته بمعونة القرائن كما في هذه الأمثلة علا أنه لا امتناع في حمل اليوم في الأول على بياض النهار، ويعلم الحكم في الليل بدليل العقل، وفي الثاني يمكن حمله على مطلق الوقت ، ويحصل التقييد من الإضافة، كما إذا قال : أنت طالق حين يصوم فلان، أو حين تنكسف الشمس.
[٢] قوله : واليوم ؛ وأما الليل فلا يستعمل لا لغة ولا عرفاً في مطلق الوقت.
(۱) الأنفال : من الآية ١٦ .
(۲) «التلويح»(١ : ١٠٦).
And the day is for the daytime. (And the day is for the daytime.) (1) His statement: "And the day... etc." is the start of mentioning a principle from which many branches arise in this discussion. Two objections are raised against it: First: They explicitly stated that "day" (اليوم) is literally for the daytime (النهار); therefore it is contrasted with night, and it is meant in their saying "a day with a night". And it is used metaphorically for absolute time, as in Allah's saying: "And whoever turns his back to them on the day..." (al-Anfal 8:16) — indeed the meaning of "day" here is absolute time, because it is clear that turning away from battle and fleeing from fighting is forbidden at all times, night or day. If "day" is meant as absolute time, it necessitates combining the literal and metaphorical meanings. The answer: In the case of carrying "day" as time, the speech is taken only in its metaphorical meaning, not combining literal and metaphorical, for it does not intend both the daytime and night, but absolute time, which is a metaphorical meaning, and its inclusion of daytime does not necessitate the prohibited combination. Second: This principle is contradicted in many cases. Often the action extends while "day" is for absolute time, such as: "Ride on the day the enemy comes to you" and "Think good of Allah on the day death comes to you." And often the action does not extend while "day" is like the brightness of daytime, such as: "You are divorced on the day Zayd fasts" and "You are free on the day the sun eclipses." The answer, as mentioned by al-Taftazani in al-Talwih and others: The mentioned ruling is only when the term is unrestricted and free from impediments and contextual clues; so it is not impossible to contravene it with the aid of contextual clues as in these examples. Moreover, there is no impossibility in carrying "day" in the first (example) as brightness of daytime, and the ruling for night is known by rational evidence, and in the second it can be carried as absolute time, and the restriction comes from the idafa (construct phrase), as if he said: "You are divorced when Zayd fasts" or "when the sun eclipses." [2] His statement: "And the day": As for night (الليل), it is not used, neither linguistically nor customarily, for absolute time.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling regarding the interpretation of the word 'اليوم' (day) in divorce and similar legal contexts. The commentator establishes a principle: when used without qualification, 'اليوم' refers to the daytime (النهار), not the full 24-hour day. This is a linguistic reality. Two objections are raised: first, that it would sometimes require combining literal and metaphorical meanings (e.g., Quranic verse where 'day' means absolute time). The answer is that that is purely metaphorical usage, not a combination. Second, the principle seems contradicted by examples like 'ride on the day the enemy comes' where the action extends beyond daytime. The answer, quoting al-Taftazani, is that the rule applies in the absence of contextual clues; with clues, it can be overridden. The commentator also notes that 'الليل' (night) is never used for absolute time.

Key Terms
اليوم (al-yawm): In legal rulings (like divorce), when used absolutely, it means the daytime (النهار), not the full 24-hour cycle.
النهار (al-nahar): The daylight period from dawn to sunset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule in this passage, what is the literal meaning of 'اليوم' in divorce statements?
A) The full 24-hour day including night.
B) Only the daytime (النهار).
C) Absolute time regardless of day or night.
D) The period from sunrise to sunset only.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, 'اليوم' in divorce oaths literally means the daytime, and contextual clues determine whether a broader meaning is intended.
